RATING: 7
it's a grand strategy take on WarCraft III, only with demons instead of orcs. And instead of trying to simply destroy everyone else's base, you have to find the dragonballs before the other players do (6 of them anyway). I'm not really familiar with the setting, but it seems like your normal high fantasy fare, with every hexagonal acre of land having either a dungeon, a dragon, or a town of evil sorcerers on it.

I think I would like it more if heroes interacted more with armies. And if the event/combat deck didn't make me feel like I should be card counting.

RATING: 7
It is a good game and many aspects of it feel well designed. The heroes are important and can win you the day but for me the hero aspect feels a little like a second layer of game pasted on the base. Strangely, I don't mind this while playing but when I think about the game afterwards it strikes me as a not so smooth kind of integration. 
Combat is tense and fun and the 4 races are interesting and play differently. Unfortunately, some races are just better (or rather: more interesting?) than others. It might not hugley affect your chances of winning but it does to some degree and it also affects the fun you get out of the game. 
The order cards combined with the season mechanism is very good design and makes for interesting decisions.

RATING: 6
Fantastic miniatures and great concept to use action cards to dictate what a faction is going to do next. Beats lots of game using a standard set of play sequences. This idea used in this game is pretty good.

Ultimately, it's like Risk game without the dicing part. All using a deck of cards with a predetermined set of results. Players that are not tired will card count. The game also mixes the elements of world of warcraft card power-ups to give flavor. Unfortunately, this often ends up having a very cluttered table. ( not an issue, but cards get flipped away sometimes.) 

Because of variable powers, it's impossible to know what your troops can do.....this is actually the good part of the game. 

An upgraded Risk game that is both fun and re-playable for all gamers. 

May not be for Wargamers that wants more control.

Much better than Eclipse (Sci Fi look-a-like) in terms of playable fun and senseless battles.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Runewars is a game that I have...complicated feeling about. So let's start at the beginning:

QUALITY:
Typical great FFG quality on components. Nice minis for the price (about Kemet quality), crisp linen cards with satisfying coatings, thick linen chipboard everywhere, hero minis, and tons more, All very good quality, with the minor nitpick that the map tiles were overly-matte feeling, like they weren't coated enough maybe.

UI/UX:
The design of the game is what can be described as typical early '10s Fantasy design, with wrought-iron and gold filigree everywhere. Obviously, this is inspired by other older Rune* games and gets a pass. It does the job, looks how you expect it, and doesn't get in the way. The Fate cards are a bit cramped but that's understandable since they embody every randomized check in the game.

RULES:
Terrible. The rules themselves make sense to a degree but they're written terribly. There was no careful thought to how it would be presented to a first-time audience at all. The first thing you do in this game is set up the board. That would seem normal except setting it up is an ordeal of:
1. Take tiles assigned to you randomly
2. Place those tiles strategically not to advantage any player unilaterally, in turn, after other players.
3. Have the first-player mark where home tiles are allowed to be placed next (I split; you choose mechanic) then everyone selects a home strategically and places their tile in a specific orientation.
4. Place your starting units in your controlled areas strategically.
5. Place your starting stronghold strategically.

All before you even know how units move/attack, what strongholds even do, what the icons on any hex represent, or what monsters will be spawning on any of them. The first impression for this game was permanently ruined to a catastrophic level as we threw pieces around randomly, feeling cheated out of what was clearly intended to be some intense mind-battle with other players. It set the tone for the entire game overwhelmingly negative. The designers NEED to add a starting board setup for 2/3/4 players with starting unit suggestions and an explanation of what to expect from each players' surrounding tiles in italic tipboxes. In fact it just needs more tipboxes in general; such as not being so attached to getting Supremacy bonuses eary on. Things you can only learn by BLINDLY poking at the game for 2 straight hours until it starts to make sense; except instead of going "ah hah!" you just go "FUCK. Guys, this move I made 8 turns ago pretty much screwed me" and everyone else looking and going "FUCK, I did that too". All this because of shit rules. 

You will waste hours reading, re-reading them (after forgetting the earlier rules), referecing them, re-referencing them, trying to make heads or tails of the setup rules, and ultimately feel like there's zero strategic depth to the game because no one knew enough to actually give it any. This is a game that heavily relies on the players being knowledgeable and strategic at literally every turn for any depth to exist.

GAMEPLAY:
Overall the gameplay is as expected from the cover and plays similarly to Kemet and Twilight Imperium, like a cross-breed: Make dudes on a map, move dudes on a map, fight dudes on a map, control points on a map, win game. There are a few nuances shoved in there like allying the monsters that spawn or completing quests with heroes but that's the gist. It's quite fun and quite a good premise once the game lets down its hair (read: once players have played for endless hours and finally understand the basic implications of actions).

Where it falls apart for me here is the constant all-pay bidding. This, for me is a complete dealbreaker and I don't have many things in games I can legitimately and without jest call "dealbreaker" more than an all-pay bid. What's an all-pay bid? In Game Theory terms, it's basically an auction where every bidder pays what they bid, regardless if they win the auction. It's incredibly stupid and actually a solved type of game with only two outcomes: Is the [thing] optional? If Yes: All bid 0. If No: Player with the most resources bids all, rest bid 0. I'm sure Corey thought players would try to outsmart each other by paying different amounts than this (and IRL, that's how people unfamiliar with the solution THINK you do it; because people are bullheaded and think they're so clever) but in reality, mathematics says it's not interesting. You bid all or nothing. And many times, it's over a victory point so you just bid all if you have the most resources. It's basically just a heavily draining tax on the rich (which makes them win faster) and changes everyone's playstyle to spamming influence gain for Winter; at least in the case of the Winter dragon rune bid. Every other bid in this goddamn game is all-pay using influence so it's hard to argue there's any real depth or interest here. It's the largest flaw in the game, worse than the lack of proper first-time setup rules; because all-pay bids are a completely broken mechanic (and illegal in the real world) and have been broken and uninteresting since they were first created. Dressing it up with fantasy gems doesn't change anything.

Overall, the 8 order cards are interesting since you can only play half of them and you get bonuses as long as you play them in ascending order. Granted, most of the time half their effects are useless unless another specific order was played just before them but they're a great mechanic nevertheless and a workable alternative to the Twilight Imperium public strategy draft (let's be honest, if that was in Runewars it would be another all-pay bid). Since it's simultaneous action selection, it adds another layer of depth in theory. In practice, no one really cares what anyone else is doing unless attacks are happening.

One good thing in the game is how it seems to welcome open player diplomacy by allowing players to trade hero treasures and influence (etc) for favors or other goods in the game. I love stuff like this because it really brings the table alive. It's a bit rarer in Runewars than TI3/4 just because there's more open and constant conflict in this game, but it's still there and very appreciated.

A prevailing thing that needs to change in this game is the never-ending BLIND payments/bidding. Not just the all-pays, but even when you try to ally neutral monsters (aka "diplomacy", though I've never understood how diplomacy is blind random chance). Since everything is resolved by this elegant fate deck, you get to spend those precious influences for more draws in the deck to diplomaticize monsters. However, there are only 4 successes in the 30-card deck. Which is another flaw...

The Fate deck is not very well executed. The idea is pretty good: draw X cards (where X is equal to units in battle, or a skill being tested by a hero, or influence spent for diplomacy, or really goddamn anything in this game) and check the correct area of the card for your resolution. Outside of combat, which unlike some other reviewers I actually believe is simple, elegant and quite well-executed; many of the other checks are just a goddamn nightmare. Unless your skill check for your Hero's quest allows both the success AND neutral case to get the treasure, you're probably taking a negative outcome. 4/30 chance is not good and it gets worse if other players have revealed more of them sooner than average. Worse, the diplomacy has you blind-bidding for cards. I haven't been entirely honest about the 4/30 since really that's just the *good* result; you can technically get a neutral result which isn't bad. But in a "war" game, you generally only take actions that are advantageous for you from an overwhelming standpoint before attempting actions with a chance at neutrality. That's called a FOOS or First-Order-Optimal strategy.

CONCLUSION:
Should you buy Runewars? I think if you enjoy TI3 for the expansion and strategic nature, it's a great buy. If you go for the political side of the game, maybe consider a different game that scratches the itch at a shorter time frame. If you haven't played Twilight Imperium, don't mind longer games with some obvious flaws, and really want a deep area control game, Runewars probably has Star Wars Rebellion beat (especially since it can suit 4 players and has more interesting faction asymmetry). The factions all seem relatively balanced, save for the horrifically overpowered Latari Elves (who win practically every one of the numerous ties in the game until a Wizard's Council who isn't them exists). The units are all cool and interesting. Fights can be dynamic and multidimensional, though once one starts there isn't a whole lot you can change.

If you're willing to put in some (a lot of) effort, Runewars can very well become a favorite. And if you're a Rune* game fan, this should probably be on your shelf at some point.

RATING: 6
Somehow I expected Runewars to be more than a Columbia block war game (card-driven action, initiative, different capacities to take damage) seen through a Fantasy Flight glass (cards, loads of them, nice plastic miniatures, bloated rulebook).  

That's a little unfair. The fate deck is a clever idea, giving the different unit types their own feel with a minimum of fiddle.  The sequencing of orders is quite challenging, with the need to balance board situation with a desire to get supremacy bonuses, and whatever the season's event has thrown at you.  The game is far from a simple slugfest; influence auctions and questing heroes are at least as important as grabbing territory.

On the debit side, the modular board set-up reminded me of the random distribution of resources in Nexus Ops.  Resources you need within easy reach are the launchpad for the win, nothing much infront of your troops makes for a painfully slow start.  Those (nearly) impassable mountains also do much to determine who you can attack and where.

In the good in parts category is the limited game length.  Through this, Runewars keeps playing time manageable and avoids endless turns of bash the leader.  However, I've had the experience shared by several commenters of the game fizzling out just as it seemed to be reaching a climax after quite a slow start.

Component quality is what we've come to expect from FFG.  Artwork is both quite good and entirely functional.  Do yourself a favour and download Universal Head's rules summary.

RATING: 8
A fun fantasy setting TI with Heroes instead of Politics.

A solid game all around, I like how the fate deck gives you some information on the odds you have of drawing a success out of a given action, but the game is not without its faults. Notably, heroes determine whether a game is won or lost, but they can really only be countered by other heroes. There needs to be some way for basic troops to attempt to hunt down heroes. Even something as fiddly as being able to spend influence during a mobilize to attempt to "see" a hero and battle it - as it is, heroes determine too much of the game while having too few weaknesses.

Game is best played with two players - larger games too often lead to troop standoffs while the heroes run around and do their business.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
After 5 plays of this game, I have to say Runewars is an incredible game.

Let's go down the list.

Components:  I'm not much of a miniatures guy, I simply just don't care what represents my army on its path of annihilation.  That being said, this game has some nice miniatures with some being a bit brittle.  The hero miniatures are awesome though.  The components overall...great.  Cards are nice (they're cards, not too hard to screw that up) and the army resource dial thingy is superb (tough cardboard). 

Difficulty:  The game is fairly convoluted, but you'll be able to pick it up after 1 or 2 plays.

Strategic Depth: Awesome.  Focusing on Dragon Runes is important but maintaining a large and healthy army is needed to acquire the Dragon Runes.  Meanwhile, you need to use your heroes effectively for scouting and completing quests.  There's a lot to this game and the constantly changing map varies the strategy each play.

Gameplay: Really fun!  The season system is a great way to keep the game pace moving, to vary turn from turn, and to keep the player thinking ahead.  The card system for choosing your action is another exciting mechanic.  Army battling is unique and a nice deviation from dice rolling.  Dueling is OKAY, but it's not a huge focus anyways; although one would be wise to make sure his heroes are fit and ready for any future duels.

Theme: Artwork is very good and the theme for each team is well represented.  Each team has something a unique inherent dynamic mechanic at play.

Overall I'm really impressed with this game.  I hope for an expansion, although that'd be a bit hard to do.  Let's hope FFG finds a way because this is a killer game.  The price is steep, no doubt, but it's well worth it.

RATING: 7
Nagyon tetszik a játékrendszer, és sok jó ötlet van a játékban. A 8 kártyánkból minden évszakban 4-et használhatunk, de bizonyos sorrendben kijátszva többet érnek a kártyák, ez  egy nagyon elegánsan megoldott dilemma. A játék legszuperebb újítása a "harvest", amivel a rendelkezésünkre álló erőforrások pillanatnyi állapotát menthetjük, és az erőforrások termelése ez alapján történik. Ez a rész kiugróan jó szerintem!!! A grafika is bejött, nincs túlbonyolítva, cserébe viszont nyelvfüggőek a kártyák. Az egységek kialakítása is tetszik (talpak alakja), és jó, hogy egy erősebb egység általában lassabb, tehát a leggyengébb egység is jó lehet valamire... A szabály meglepően egzakt és nem is túl hosszú (kevesebb, mint egy óra). Még hangulata is van a játéknak, pedig nem vagyok egy rúna fan. Ami külön tetszett, hogy nem csak ész nélkül magunk előtt gyűjtögetjük a rúnákat, hanem hexánként csak 1 rúna lehet a pályán, így a sok rúna birtoklásához nagy terület is kell. Szintén nagy ötlet az, hogy a 6 rúna (ez a végcél) bejelentése után 1 évig meg is kell azt tartani a győzelemhez! Csak úgy bemondásra nem lehet nyerni. Az utolsó 1 év élethalálharc, nagyon izgalmas! A 4 faj elég jól kiegyenlítettnek tűnik, épp azért, mert nincsenek rossz egységek. Amelyik gyenge, az cserébe gyors.
De hogy ne csak jót mondjak: a játék elég hosszú. Minden év kb. 1 óra volt hármunknak, szerintem 4-en még a haladóknak is van ennyi. És 5-6 évnél hamarabb nehezen lehet vége (7 év a max). A hosszához képest viszont durván szerencsefüggő. A szerzett rúnák döntő többségét közvetlenül valamelyik kihúzott kártyán kapjuk. Van akinek 10-ből 0 ilyen jut, van akinek 10-ből 5... Tehát nem igazán érzem, hogy tenni lehet érte, inkább vakszerencse... A másik, hogy az évszak lapok (és általában az előforduló kártyák) ismerete szerintem nagyon fontos, ezek ismerete ill. nem ismerete óriási előny/hátrány lehet! Megdöbbentő, hogy milyen erős lapok vannak, ahhoz  képest, hogy 6 megszerzett rúna a végfeltétel, nagyon könnyen lehet hozzájuk jutni, de könnyen el is veszthetők. A játék hossza és nehézsége nem áll arányban ezzel a random szinttel. És végül: nekem a hősös dolog nem tetszik, mert a hősök kis küldetései kicsit erőltetettek, és feleslegesen sok szerencse kerül így a játékba. A rúnák java részét ők hozzák be.

RATING: 7
After one mostly completed game (5 runes)

Good stuff: 
- Not too complex, 
- multiple tracks to improve
- not too unit dense, 
- avoids analysis paralysis by keeping choices straightforward and goals mostly clear

Not so good:
- Might be difficult to get a handle on which decision are actually helpful and which aren't (board changes less than one might expect in this genre)
- Luck might be a little too heavy for length of play
- Rulebook might not be up to the task of the game play.

Overall:
- Seems more solid than a lot of games in this genre
- Managing questing heroes in addition to armies was enjoyable and theme-y (the overlap between both was enjoyable also
- I thought the factions and resources were well done without being unnecessarily tedious to track
- The fate deck was a nice alternative to dice, but I did miss the tactile experience of rolling dice

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Deep, complex and thematic, Runewars is everything you can wish for in a game, even though sometimes it slows too much and in the end lasts way too long. 

Anyhow, the one real big flaw I found in Runewars regards the heroes: they just feel like a late and forced addition to the game as they’re disconnected from the rest of the events. They simply wander through the map looking for quests (and they can really do the difference here) but otherwise have no other real use in the game: in fact they don’t join regular units, take no interest in battles and don’t assist you in anything you’re doing. They simply don’t care.

The game could also greatly benefit from a wider number of season cards to make every game really different and unpredictable rather than just slightly varying the order they appear. (December 2012)

[b]Update[/b] - After playing with the revised rules I’ve noticed most of my concerns about the original game have been worked out: most important, not only are the heroes better integrated into the game now but they also play a major role in battles, as each of them allows a specific unit to draw one more fate card, which is both thematically appropriate and a huge aid to turn the battle into one’s favor.

Another big improvement is the prospective winner now has to state he’s collected six runes, show where they are and hold them over a whole year in order to get his victory: on the one hand it causes a «bash the leader» endgame, sure, but on the other hand it also prevents the much worse «the onlooker gets the best of a fight» effect from suddenly occurring.

Since the revised rules really improve on the way the game plays, in May 2013 I raised my rating from 8 to 9: although very long (and likely a bit longer than before), Runewars is a very enjoyable game now.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Rating after one 4-player game. Epic for sure. In the parlance of Joe Huber, I think there is "lots to do", in only a max of 24 proper actions. Different directions to go. Each event is something to do or react to, and the orders are typically multi-faceted. I was pretty happy with the lack of downtime as well, with the threaded turns, and simultaneous events. Afterward I was thinking about the different ways I could have played it. The combat system is interesting, and quick, which is a nice combo. I had worries about the heroes not getting to do much, but really, there is plenty to do in the game already. The "supremacy bonus" system is very interesting for creating some competing incentives for orders. Also, since there are several paths for victory (battle, bids, and heroes) and several supplementary mechanics (cities, developments, tactics cards, objectives) you can focus on just a few, almost ignoring others. This is pretty cool, in that everyone isn't doing the same stuff all the time. Plus, the asymmetrical factions really have different feels, based on their armies, dials, and available developments. I really want to play again, and I also think 2-player will work really well. Good stuff.

Edit: Played a 2-player game, and it worked very well. Map setup will determine whether you have a bottle neck, or a multi-front war. If my next game is as fun as my last 2, this might go to a 9.

Edit: Played a 3-p with exploration tokens. They are a nice addition, not too complex, adding flavor, but adding a bit of time. After a few plays, the heroes are integrated into the game just fine. Lost this game when I lost about 4 battles by a few points each. By the end, I had almost nothing left at all. Still fun though. I think this is going to stay an 8 for me.

RATING: 8
Runewars is the sort of full-bodied, epic-fantasy game that does a lot in four short hours. Four distinct armmies and their heroes stride across a world full of magic, creatures, and treasures. Everything is handled through several sets of initiative and combat resolution decks that allow players to fight battles with surprising results. The  figures are also a treasure: many different types and plenty of them. All geared for players familiar with the Runebound-Descent universe. If you missed out on the old TSR Classic "Divine Right", then don't let Runewars get by you!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7.5
Juego ambientado en el universo de Descent. Hay cuatro razas (Elfos, humanos, no muertos y uthuk) que deben competir por ser los primeros en obtener el control de 6 runas. 

El tablero es modular y cada partida será diferente. Cada jugador controla una raza, y dos runas iniciales. Hay varias formas de obtener runas. Cada jugador durante la partida, también puede controlar uno o varios héroes que le permitan realizar aventuras que pueden tener como premio una forma alternativa de conseguir runas.

Es un juego muy entretenido y con muchas variables en todo momento. Cada turno representa una estación. Si en 6 años (24 turnos) nadie ha conseguido controlar las 6 runas, el juego termina igualmente.

Como siempre ocurre con fantasy flight games, materiales  y presentación sobresalientes.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
As a fan of the Heroes of Might and Magic series on the PC growing up (my sister and I played nos. 1-3 to death), I was sold on Runewars as soon as it became available.  Modular boards, unique races with distinct units, fortresses, resource management, questing... it was all there.  What brings it all to another level for me is that this is basically a Euro hybrid.  Ameritrash wrappings, for sure, but the mechanisms that run this game are pure Euro.  The way the fate deck works, along with the one-per-season actions, assures that you have to take care sequencing what you do, and provides a very nicely balanced set of limits.  Do I take the action I need now, and give up future bonuses?  Do I push forward with that assault now, when it is advantageous for me to do so, even though the fates might be against me?  Such questions arise in dice-fests too, but the cycling of the seasons in this one adds a level of predictability and structure that is a hallmark of many Euros.  The seasons also prevent the game from going on too long - indeed, every play we've had of this (except for one game that was a bit of a route) has ended before we were ready.

If you come to this expecting a slug-fest, you might be disappointed.  Yes, there is combat in here, but the battles are less than frequent.  This is fine with me, because it makes battling more meaningful, it gives the game some weight and narrative pull.  This game throws in a little of everything, but blends all these elements to the point of perfection.  All we need now are expansions - more races and units would be nice for sure, but definitely more cards and land pieces!  This game (and many of Konieczka's designs) should become landmarks, simply because they can satisfy all kinds of gamers, from the light to the serious, from the fan of plastic and conflict to those introspective Euro lovers.  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 8.5
UPDATE:  Played a whole bunch of games.  Want to keep playing it more and more.

Runewars is so, so good.  Represents all that I love about tabletop gaming, but keeps the playtime in a good range.  Played four-player, which is the right amount to ensure that you don't end up in a two-gang-up-on-one three-player situation.  Will happily play with two or four in the future.  Would play the game again, right now, if I could.

Orders mixed with a random map, assymetrical forces, flavor, theme, a little chrome, good bits and some interesting mini games, but all in a digestible format.  Yikes, what a great mix.

I never thought a fantasy conquest game could knock off Wizard Kings... was I wrong?  Too early to tell, but I sure do love Runewars.

Can't wait for expansions, as long as the expansions offer additional nations/races to play.

All that said ONE CAVEAT - I don't like the fate deck for diplomacy... counting the cards, marking off the positive outcomes.  It takes a little bit of the fun and adventure out of the whole thing.  

UPDATE:  I think the fate deck works great for combat and questing.  It's a stroke of genius, really.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Two plays. Once as the Undead and once as the Elves. There is a lot to like in this game but there are also some questionable design choices that I think could be improved upon. None of the mechanics are difficult but the game feels rather cumbersome and overburdened with too much stuff going on.

What I liked: Multi-player fantasy. The theme. The nice hex pieces with fantastic integrated moutain ranges. The armies and neutral miniatures were quite well done. I liked the order card mechanic with four cards being chosen each year (one per season) and the lowest ones going first and receiving a bonus if the highest yet played. It produced the right kind of excruciating decisions that I love about gaming.

What I didn't like: The large number of decks of cards with occasional hugely overpowered cards. The fate cards should be scrapped and replaced with a d20 or d30 as the deck is too small causing the odds to change considerably between draws (see files section for creative solution). The 'heroes' didn't add anything to the gameplay, just another gamey/random way of earning a dragon rune or two. I wonder if they were shoehorned into the game because of FFG's previous games. The scale of the hero miniatures also makes them look as if they were just tacked on as an afterthought. I would happily chuck out the heroes and their quests and make this a military conflict game. It would have been nice to have stronghold miniatures instead of the rather plain cardboard tokens to match the great 3D mountains but that is a very minor complaint and I'm sure it would be possible to find some from another game (such as Kings & Things). The ability to ally with neutral monsters was game-breakingly easy (for those with influence) and must surely need some house rules to take into account the number and type of monsters being encountered? Finally, why is the box so big and the icons on cards, etc, so small?

RATING: 7.6
Runewars is a very ambitious game which tries to combine the element of area control, wargame with mission-based RPG system, results in a complex and time-consuming 4x(explore, expand, exploit, exterminate)game. The game takes seven turns(years) with four phases(seasons) per turn, which means an entire afternoon plus maybe half of evening in real world. 
  
Despite of its exhausting gaming experiences, Runewars is actually an interesting game with four distinguished races, various units and random map which make you want to play it again and again...only if you have enough time and willing to spend.

RATING: 9
This is the game I find myself pulling out these days when I used to pull out Twilight Imperium. TI is still a solid 10 in my book, but it just takes so long to play and it's difficult to get people to commit an entire day to it.

Runewars manages to distill the essence of TI and turn it into a very smooth running game you can play in two-three hours. Also, it looks smashing!

Update: Just played my first game using the Epic variant yesterday. People around here claim it's the way the game should be played. I don't think I agree. We played for over five hours and had only made it through five of the eight years before having to call it quits.

For me, one of the best things about Runewars is that you can get kind of a Twilight Imperiumesque experience in two-three hours. If you use the Epic variant this goes out the window and Runewars becomes just as long as TI and then... you might as well play TI! :)

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
I like this game very much. This game fits perfectly the complex-strategic-conquer-the-world-or-do-shrewd-diplomacy genre. I like to play it. Actually I like Twilight Imperium 3rd ed. more, but this game is much shorter and almost as complex. Each play lasts about four hours with four players even if some of the players have to think long on some of their turns.

The different factions in this game are not perfectly in balance in my opinion but closely so. I've won with all of them even though some of them work better as aggressors than others and my playstyle is often very aggressive. In recent games I've concentrated more on farming influence and then using it to generate Dragon Runes to win the game and I've found this method to be very efficient. What a shocking finding.

Pros:
- Plastic mountains. Say what you will, but I have the original edition with the plastic mountains and they rock! Pardon the possible pun. I like to push around figurines in these kinds of strategy games and the more the merrier.
- Map generation. I'm totally in love with how the map is generated from larger pre-assembled hex combinations for each play. 

Cons:
- The heroes. Their involvement in the game is not perfect. The expansion tackles this a bit but still not enough. I think they could use another questing phase during each game year. Games usually end during the sixth year and this means that the heroes get to act only five to six times during the game. This makes the training action for the heroes sub-optimal.
- Diplomacy. In this game you don't really have anything extra you could trade with the other players. Everybody ends up being a mortal enemy of every other player since turn 1.

RATING: 10
Wow...what a game!

A superb blend of tactics, heros, area control, and luck (love it or hate it....I love a bit of luck in my games). A truly great blend of fantasy, battles, heroes and some luck. No two games are ever the same with Runewars. There is plenty of strategy, tactics and luck and you have to be a gamer who is adept at modifying your strategy based on the current game conditions.

Most players either love or hate the game. From a family standpoint, there is much to offer. We've played individual games and had fun, and also tried some team varieties to help teach my younger kids (5 to 8) the game. The end result, win or lose, is that everyone really likes the 'whole' of the game. The look, the flow, the battles, the strategy...all have enjoyed it. 

It is the best game ever? *shrugs* Who cares! It is a fun, deep yet easy to learn game that serious gamers and families alike can enjoy. A must buy in my book !!!!!

RATING: 8
Runewars has variable board setup that I like.  All players participate in forming the world as they like it.  The cards that are drawn before each season also makes sure that every year (round) in the game is unique.  You do need to plan a little ahead, as the activation markers limit your movements (as Twilight Imperium).  Quests range from simple to impossible, based on game conditions.  So luck of the draw can make some of the cards in your hand useless.  This was not too much trouble though, as it seemed to balance out.  Battles are interesting, but they can also be very costly for both sides.  The game is a little long, but my only complaint is the half-sized cards.  Runwars is a fun time, and has lots of replay value.

RATING: 8
I love this game!  It's like playing Warcraft III in boardgame format:  You collect resources, build strongholds and developments, recruit soldiers to battle enemies and neutral units, engage in combat, collect treasures -- it's got it all.  There's also a completely separate element where you control heroes who go on quests and duel other heroes, which is also really fun.  The components are top notch -- the figures look great, the art is some of the best I've seen in a game, and there are tons are cool tokens and cards.

The only thing that can potentially suck is how luck-based the combat can be, since you randomly draw cards, but it's still better than just rolling dice. Knocking some points off because it's so complex that we never play it.

RATING: 6
[2013-11-11 Rating: 7.3 -> 6]
After playing Runewars again, I feel the need to adjust my rating. This isn't because there was some flaw in it that I discovered recently, it's just that my gaming preferences have changed since I had last played it. [2010-07-10 Rating: n.a. -> 7.3]
Runewars is a quite epic game playtime-wise. Every player controls one race with some distinct abilities. Over the course of the game (which seems to last about one hour per player) you build your kingdom on a modular map, hire some heroes and attack your opponents. You do all this in order to be first the one to possess six dragon runes and win the game. If no one manages to get six dragons runes after six years, the game ends with the winner being the player with the most dragon runes.
The players plan their actions simultaneously by using cards. This mechanism is comparable to the one used in Wallenstein or Dungeon Lords. Per year, you play four actions. So you have at max 24 actions before the game is over.

Comments:
--------------
* I enjoyed my first game. I had a lot of stuff to think about and I felt like I had some control over my destiny, despite the myriad of cards that are drawn each turn and which affect the game quite a lot. The game is fun to play. But I also had the feeling, that the game could be a lot more streamlined. One of my favorite "wargames" is Wallenstein. And Runewars reminded me a lot of it. In some regard, Runewars seemed to be the more complicated brother. And I'm not sure sure that the additional layers (like the tactic cards) made the game better. Because of the easier gameplay of Wallenstein, you discover the important things that are needed after the first play. From this point on, the player who manages their actions better wins. I'm not sure yet, how long it takes until I reach this point in Runewars. There seems to be too much chrome. 

* I'm not sure whether I like the fixed resolving order of the action cards. Recruiting always comes after Attacking, so you can't add strength to your lands before an attack if you forgot it last year. While I can see why this design decision was made, I really like Dirk Henn's more flexible solution of the same problem in Wallenstein.

* The long playing time might be a show stopper.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Oy... how many game mechanisms can you fit it one box? Waaay too many...

There was one deck of cards, the ones that gave my race special unique powers, that I never even used. I turned them over once or twice while wading through the sea of possibilities between my turns and had to check my gag reflex. Clearly the designer aimed for 'epic' and landed on merely 'huge'.

Playing with a random tile set-up it was clear that one player got hosed and another had a very favorable opening position. Hours of dissatisfying grind later, the result was exactly as predicted.

I think if you gave a copy of this to teenage boys with nothing else to do (and no other games in their collection) they could house-rule out the flaws and play it enough to actually use all, or most, of the chrome. I think I would have loved it at that age. This explains my rating of a 5 even though I'll never play it again.

RATING: 5
Long complicated diplomatic war games are not my favorite type of game.  This one is a more complicated version of Diplomacy (or Risk) set in a fantasy world.  The graphics are awesome, and the bits are cool, but the rules are overly complicated for my taste.  It seems like you could cut out nearly 25% of the rules and still get the same depth and the same sense of theme.  For starters, I would get rid of the whole Hero aspect of the game.  It is a weird subgame that doesn't belong.  I would also reduce the number of units.  Too many of the units are similarly bland (like all the different version of the triangle units).  It adds a lot of noise when learning the game, but I don't change my game play decisions depending on which triangle unit is in each space.  For strategic decision making, they are as good as being all the same.

With that said, there are many cool things going on in the game.  The core of the game is well thought out.  There was a time in my life when I would have absolutely loved the game.  (though I would have only found people to play it with on 1 or 2 occasions)

4th Play:  3 player game, 1 evil + 2 good.  After five hours we barely made a dent.

Each subsequent play I have dropped my rating by ½ a point.  I become less enamoured with each play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Although it will often be compared to TI3 (or starcraft), runewars is its own game, with a distinct style and elements not found in its other FFG big-box brethren.  Heroes and questing is one of the main things, as this gives you an alternative way to get points.  The events are another, although once you've seen them a couple times, you start to get a feel for what's coming up (there aren't many unique possibilities).  But direct conquest always remains an option.  Unlike many games where your points are stored on the VP track, safe for the whole game once earned, in this one they have to be placed on the board in a specific region.  Lose control of the region, and you lose that point.
The factions are fairly asymmetric, and it plays just fine with 3 or 4, where TI really needs 4 at a minimum and preferably 5 or 6.  You do have options for play length, as well.  The 1st edition had a short race to 6 points, starting at 2; the 2nd is still to 6 points, but you start at 0-1 and have to hold on to them for a year; and there's always the 1st ed long game if you're feeling really ambitious.  The map, made from modular tiles, is completely different from game to game.  Quests tend to be pretty same-y, but there's still a bit of excitement in seeing whether your hero can beat the odds and recover some treasure.  The actual items are kind of the least interesting part, because they get used so infrequently.  Most players seem to be unwilling to risk their hero in a deadly duel, esp. when some 3rd party could just swoop in and steal everything from the victor.  Picking on a badly-wounded hero is obviously a different matter, but it doesn't matter how much cool gear you have: you're unlikely to win if you're at 1 HP.
The staggered combat with initiative, routs, and damage is a little cumbersome, especially when the larger army will still usually trample the smaller one.  It works, though, and allows the factions some flavor in the units' special abilities and such.  It's also nice that killing guys or surviving is no guarantee of winning the battle: you have to have more strength left than your opponent, and if he's got a fortress, that's a hurdle to get over.
So, this is a fine fantasy empires game.  Lots of moving parts, but then, it's an FFG title.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A

But all in all, another game that I'm inclined to, and luckily I'm made the right purchase. Now waiting for the expansion, which allows Heroes to be commanders to lead the army!

RATING: 6

1. Heroes in many aspects are mini-game in this game. They don't interact with armies, just make some quests. It can be easily removed.

3. Heroes can earn victory points more random, than you can do it other ways. Someone can "sudenly" win, thanks to his hero and some luck in cards.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This had potential for me as being 'The Epic' without the epic playing time.  In my three games, unfortunately, the game has been either longer than I would like or short without much player interaction.  My plays may have been a bit atypical in that one game we made a triangle-like map for three players but created a huge mountain range starting in the center of the base of one of the sides going towards the center of the map which caused all of us to funnel into one area without much interaction until late game.  The second game I played was a four player game in which the game ended on either round 3 or 4 and ended before there was one army battle.  The third game, I was determined to have more player interaction so we played the 'Epic variant' and I we were a lot more careful with the map creation.  Well, I did get my player interaction but after over three hours and being on round 5 of 8 we just decided to call it quits.

The five major things I didn't like about the game were as follows:

1) I don't like the pacing in the game.  The game feels too long with the Epic variant and too short without it.

2) Map creation can mess with the game a lot, especially with inexperienced players.  There should have been some pre-made maps included within the games rules which could be used until players become familiar with the game and realize how important the map creation is to a balanced and fun game.

3) Heroes.  It's been said before but here's my take: I find the heroes phase to be jarring in that it distracts me from the more interesting resource acquisition, tile conquering game.  I understand Heroes are needed as another way of gaining Dragon Runes as well as serves other functions with tactics cards and rune scouting.  It doesn't change the fact that I didn't enjoy what could be done with them and took me away from what I did find interesting.

4) I like the neutral units in the game but wish they had been delegated to recruiting in cities and not littered all over the map.  Like Age of Conan, it bothers me that part of the early game sees little player interaction because of the neutral units that are in the way.

5) This game is very random.  Some tactics cards are amazing but others a kind of ho-hum.  The quest rewards can vary an where between very poor armor to one of your victory points!  You only need 6 to win the game!  There is strategy to building your army a certain way versus certain opponents but combat feels very random.

Overall, I'm sure I sound quite negative about the game but I still actually enjoyed playing it... for at least some of it's duration anyway.

RATING: 9
A very nice and good-looking area control fantasy game. I like the thing that this game is not a pure wargame. Your object is to gain Dragon Runes, and there are many ways to do that: a) recruit a huge army and conquer, b) gain influence for biddings, c) your heroes can accomplish quests to gain a Dragon Rune, and even d) there's one tactics card that let you place a Dragon Rune on the map. At first I found the game quite random, but when you get to know the Season events and benefits of Factions' Units better, Runewars doesn't taste that random anymore. One of my favourite games at the moment, I like Runewars a lot!

Updated 2013-11-19: Runewars, I still like it. Odd thing is that I prefer playing the base game without expansion.

RATING: 9.8
Great game, has elements from several games combined into one. I can't remember the last time I've had this much fun playing a board game, even the setup was a blast! Overall, the game is very well balanced and we ran into very few problems.

One thing I feel needs to be improved about this game is the hero dueling; it is far too simple and has no strategy involved. My biggest disappointment about hero dueling was that the hero's stats did not have any effect in combat.

Therefore, to improve hero duels, some house rules should be added to give an effect in combat based on a hero's stats. For example, strength could increase the amount of damage a hero deals, agility could make a hero more accurate (look at a different section of a fate card), and wisdom could increase the amount of cards a hero draws.

RATING: 6
It's surprising to see how elegantly all the different parts of this game work together. Very impressive design. My favorite aspect of the game is how well the heroes interact between questing and helping to conquer areas with your armies. It's also neat to be able to recruit some neutral units to your army for some additional special abilities to diversify your basic units.

Sadly, the randomness of tactic card abilities and card combat resolution frustrates me quite a bit given the length of the game. That and I just generally don't quite understand how to best engage in combat. I tend to either over commit my troops and then have to lose stuff to the "8 troop max" rule... Or under commit and am then forced to retreat a lot of my troops who wind up getting autokilled by whatever opportunistic opponent happens to be the next to act (since retreated units become routed and routed units can't defend themselves or move, therefore they all just die...) 

Ultimately, the game is just not for me, but I did enjoy the journey of it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Really nice modular board and the 3d plastic mountains are a really nice simple but effective touch. The hexes are large enough to contain all the mini's and tokens that may be required which is frequently an issue for other similar games.

The four different factions play very differently with the 'evil' factions caring less about influence and more about cards for random events and the 'good' factions more about influence and thus ability to dictate more the effect of lasting game changing events.

Within each faction there are several types of unit and this leads to several different ways to play the faction. Do you go for the elite army of few units that 'should' hit hard and fast or do you plough ahead with limitless skeletons that will keep getting up but are slow and pretty weak individually?

The combat cards are a really nice idea as it removes the die aspect. A card is turned over for each unit and dependent upon the shape of the units base dictates what that unit does this turn be it damage, magic effect, rout a unit or simple missing.

Neutral units may be fought or may be coerced into joining your army which has its risks. Quests also rely upon a similar mechanic whereby no die are rolled to see if you are successful.

We always play to a certain number of turns rather than to a certain number of dragon runes as this helps to moderate the game length.

RATING: 5
Strong game, with no major problems for me except that it's too long and requires a bit of deck knowledge.

Duration: Simultaneous orders is good, and players should be able to run their turns without too much AP. However the game runs much too long.
Design: The seasons are great, the order supremacy and production dials interesting though a bit gamey. Combat is fantastic, though swingy (leaving a routed army completely vulnerable); I don't mind the bolted on hero game. The activation token mechanic is non-intuitive at first; the titles are mostly pointless; and the neutral diplomacy swingy and theme-crinkling.
Production: The evil mountains are fantastic, the miniatures good but fragile.
Theme: It's more generic fantasy plus some demon-worshipping barbarians, but the necromancer mechanic is good. Some elements are gamey (order supremacy, influence).

Update: It takes way too long, and is ultimately a who-attacks-whom game under all the mechanics.

RATING: 9
- Twilight Imperium 3 meets Game of Thrones the Boardgame. Combines elements of both games. VERY good game and quite underrated. 
- It's not as long as TI3 but still takes 4-8 hours in a four player game.
- Plays nice with two players as well! Three players is not recommended as it seems more balanced to an even number of factions.
- The downtime is not bad if all players stay involved during the battles and help setting them up & resolve them. But if you have lazy players who get easily distracted, playing on their phone when it is not their turn etc, then the downtime during battles can be high. Just keep everyone involved during battles and it is no problem...
- Expansion is heavily recommended as it adds a lot of interesting features.
- Fate deck is awesome if you understand how it is intended to be used. (It lets you anticipate the changing probabilities of success which change during the game depending on how many cards have been used, which is really nice and adds to the fantasy setting IMHO as it gives almos a prophecy or omen like feeling...)
- Go get it if you like a good heavy wargame now an then.

RATING: 5
In many respects Runewars is very similar to Warrior Knights by the same designer. There are a number of notable differences (modular board, asymmetric player powers, and the victory conditions to name a few) but they do share quite a lot of the underlying mechanics.

Consequently, they also pretty much share the same verdict: Both are fine games but they are just too long for what they provide. The fate cards are probably the single most important factor in Runewars, and the difference between success and failure - heads or tails, basically, except failure is often much more likely - can be so ridiculously huge it's not funny.

RATING: 8
Yeah, Runewars is a pretty cool Corey K. game! I really love the look of it, and just the whole layout is super pretty. There's lots going on and many paths to victory.  Lots of delicious decisions to make as you play. Almost everything comes together in such a great way: the bidding, the Order cards, the season effects, etc. While the Quests feel like a separate thing that is not entirely integrated into the rest of it, they don't bother me too much. And combat might appear random at times (and why not use dice instead?) but I don't think there's much of a problem with the Fate deck as it stands. I think with everyone taking their turns quickly this is a wonderful game. But it can be a slog fest if there is even one player with AP. Overall, this might be my favourite epic "dudes on a map" wargame for three or four players.

RATING: 9
[b][i]Runewars[/i][/b] distills my favorite portions of Twilight Imperium and adds a few new elements to make a concentrated, though neither simple nor short, game of empires clashing. Multiple aspects of the game--such as the action selection mechanism, the battle system, and resource management--reward planning and smart play. Distributing event cards by season adds a thematic flare that I appreciate. My biggest complaint is that the heroes feel a little out of place in this war game, but this is substantially improved in the Banners of War expansion.

I could go on, but, really, these mechanical aspects are just the backdrop to an epic clash of skeletons, gryphons, and dragons; what's not to love.

RATING: 8.5
Well rated multi-faction area control fantasy game set in the Descent universe.  I picked up my copy at a local auction and glad I did.  Very reminiscent of the mechanics seen in Twilight Imperium (i.e. order cards, influence bids, secret objectives, area control) in condensed form combined with hero questing as seen in World of Warcraft the board game.  Beautiful modular board and miniatures.  Not a short game by any means, but with a strong grasp of the rules it could be played in one sitting.

Enjoying this quite a bit.  Most of the 4X I want in a much more compact package than TI:3.  Pretty fair amount of battle and I love the orders system, much better than TI:3's Puerto Rico system.  I'm seeing a number of possible paths to take to win--influence vs. battle vs. heroes and some combination of those.  Will that be enough or is one better than the other?  Time will tell.  Heroes are mainly a rune generating mechanism but that's ok with me, I didn't have any other expectations about what they should do.  Nicely differentiated sides too.

I see more combat than most seem to in my games but I really need to play Epic.  Basically everyone I speak to says it is the real game in this box.  So if the normal game is getting an 8.5 for me, I look forward to the Epic variant!  As for heroes, I find them very interactive.  In most of my games there are a huge number of duels once rewards start coming out and I have run out of heroes in most of my games.

I agree that it should have 6 factions in the box.  But what it really needs more than that are more cards.  I never thought I'd say this about an FFG game but there just aren't enough cards in this box--quest, fate, reward, hero, season, etc.  Most of the decks, save the tactics deck, are suffering from too few cards in them.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Lots of fun. Sort of a mix of TI mechanics but with a fantasy theme and more streamlined game play. Looking forward to playing again with the expansion mixed in. I'm not super crazy about the heroes but I think the expansion will fix that.

RATING: 9
The more I read about this one the more I want it.
I will definitely pick it up eventually, regardless of how expensive it is...it just looks and sounds way too cool. But, once again I have stopped myself from buying this simply because of the playing time.

Traded for Fresco and expansions.

At the very least, this looks pretty. Getting ready to dig-in.

It's a really good, solid game, and much less complex than I expected it to be. It's full of difficult decisions and often conflict happens after the first year, which keeps it from slowing down. But, it also creates even more difficult decisions.

It's what you'd expect from Runewars, and if you're looking for a good fantasy wargame/civ builder it delivers on all fronts.

The thing it could benefit most from is the addition of another race or two. Not that four isn't plenty, I'm always one for more variety in play styles to keep the game fresh for a really long time.

This one's definitely a keeper, as it scratches all the right itches when you want a good, meaty area control/conflict game.

Unless you play with AP prone players, the game clips along at a really good pace. The average 2 player game has clocked in at about 2.5 hours for me so far. With so many difficult choices presented to the player every turn, this could really bog things down if certain player types are confronted with Runewars.

RATING: 6
This game is more than a toy. While this is a typical Fantasy Flight fare with lots of decks and other components it feels more coherent than older epic FFG games, like Twilight Imperium and Warrior Knights. Actually, I think this game in many places will replace Warrior Knights as while it has the same feel is nicer looking and is more engaging. There are many things to explain when playing it the first time, but the mechanics are pretty straightforward. After you have chosen what to play during this round, most actions are pretty obvious, even though it is quite often disrupted by events.

I see a positive trend with Fantasy Flight Games with this and Chaos in the old world. The games are shorter in play time, while still feeling epic (maybe in both cases the games should last a little longer than they do). They both seems to be pretty balanced, well at least there isn’t a critical flaw with it.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Pros: 
- the game after setup moves surprisingly fast, and faster than Twighlight Imperium, the game you cannot avoid to make comparisons with; 
- it gives you the idea of vast armies marching and amassing, clashing in spectacular battles (combat is very so better than TI3);
- it has many levels of interaction, Tactic Cards are important, more so Influence, and the "x" weapon, the Dragons or the Giants you can actually try to recruit!

Cons: 
- initial placement and map conformation are too important. A player who is in the wrong path of two other players or in the path of just one, while the other two amass riches, is DOOMED. There is no amount of dealing and wheeling that can save you from certain elimination. Thus house rules for the map making need to be devised.
- the hero mechanic seems just "placed" upon the game, with no real interaction with the true action. I didn't feel compelled to go and hunt the other players' heroes as they were a little threat compared to the massive armies that were about to clash. They could have used a "War of the Ring" approach, where heroes may command armies and do stuff that is truly meaningful. 

Random thoughts: 
- as with Twilight Imperium, the game is not about combat, but about making the more "points" to end the game. But there are two things that make it better: 1) you can gain more runestones in one turn without having to choose from a certain order to get more than one per turn; and 2) you may have to attack or use heroes to take the runestones from the territories of other players, thus making a war worth fighting for.  

What do I think: 
It is a game I'd give a solid "7". I'm usually willing to play this game, but I don't care much if I don't play it and I'll play it only if other people come up with the idea. 
It is better and cleaner than Twilight Imperium, so if you like that you will simply LOVE Runewars. 
If you do not love TI3, you still will think of the game as a very good design despite it may not be your cup of tea.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This could be great, the ultimate Fantasy warfare game, with the beautiful art and fantastic miniatures that you expect from FFG. But unfortunately it also comes with their usual excess of tokens, card decks and rules. I enjoyed my first play but was not very impressed.

I liked the action selection system. You want to play your cards in a certain order to get bonuses, but that order will not always be the best for your plans or to react to a game situation. The other mechanics, however, bring nothing new or particularly interesting.

There is an attempt to look original in the card-driven combat, but it is just a gimmick. There is no hand management, as you just take cards from the deck and check the results for the corresponding unit types. It is good that it is fast, but it could have been easily implemented with dice.

The luck factor seems excessive. The "diplomacy" mechanic used to gain control of neutral creatures is terrible. The relative forces of your army and the creatures mean nothing. You just commit influence points to flip cards, and if you are lucky to get one of the four good cards in the whole deck, the creatures ally with you. If not, you just wasted your valuable influence for nothing.

You win by controlling more runes than your opponents, and heroes are the most important means of bringing those runes to the board. That is awfully dependent on sheer luck, and the heroes feel completely detached from the rest of the game, barely helping in battles. The game might actually be better without them.

A few other elements could also be left out and would not be missed. I fail to see the purpose, for example, of the title cards, just one more thing to keep in mind that adds nothing to the gameplay. And yet another huge luck factor comes in the form of tactics cards, which carry all sorts of surprises. Of course, they are also largely unbalanced and their usefulness depends on circumstances. But unfortunately this bloated approach to game design is the modus operandi of this Corey guy.

All in all, although somewhat spoiled by excessive luck and complexity, it is not a bad game. It just had the potential to be so much better! But then again, my rating is probably a little generous, because the game is so beautiful and I'm a huge fan of Fantasy.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

[i]Spies![/i]

[i]Assassins![/i]

[i]Agents For The Win![/i]

RATING: 6

The relationship between battling for control of a hex & winning the game with 6 runes is not straightforward - oftentimes a battle will take place, one player wins & we kind of feel, "Is that it?"

I prefer Warrior Knights to this.

The tie-in with Runebound is nice & the heroes really do add a (minor) level of "adventure game" (although the hero part does seem to interrupt the flow of the game a bit).

Also, the board is very pretty!

And it's quite good as a 2 player.

Update: Played the Epic game with a few players who were more enthusiastic & really enjoyed it.  It's still very long & a bit fiddly but that session has halted my falling opinion of it.

Further update: Played another 4 player epic & I'm just not sure this is worth the effort.  I could fit in quite a few better games with the time & effort required for this.

Extra change in Revised (for Elves): Word of Vaal: Your opponent must retreat one unit of your choice from the battle.

Note provisoire 26 nov 2016 j'ai commencé à apprendre le jeux.  Au début on est un peu perdu avec les saison et les cartes d'ordre.  Je commence à comprendre un peu la synchronicité entre les deux qui devient plus important plus tard dans le jeu.

Les ressources c'est bon avec les plateaux individuelles de les resetter et on ne perd pas sans faire un autre reset.  Difficile d'atteindre la grosse bête fait que une chance qu'il y a les neutres que tu peux ramasser.

Je pense que je pourrais mettre un 9 facile car on travaille pas mal pour gérer les choses.  Je vais voir avec l'expansion.  J'aime bien Kemet mais runewars ajoute beaucoup de choses pour mieux étoffer.  C'est sur que les parties sont plus longues mais je commence à comprendre ce que les gens parle quand on mentionne le côté narratif du jeu.

Pi tant qu'à faire on réduit le nombre d'année si on veux des parties plus courte ou le nombre de rune à trouver.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
2012-10-08

Initial Rating: 8.5 (September 2012)

I really like Runewars, it is perhaps the best game I have played in the Terrinoth realm :D. Konieczka has designed some very good games.

It is a big game with lots of cool components which I really love. Also, the art is gorgeous. The game can be long (my plays have been 6 hours and 4 hours) but as I´m immersed in it I don’t mind, and for the same reason, I didn’t feel any downtime. For me, it is very reminiscent of Twilight Imperium (I still prefer TI3). It has a lot of luck, but in most situations you can react to it so I´m not bothered (much) by it. I must confess it bothers me a bit when we draw the Special goals and the heroes missions. It seems the luck of the draw can offer a lot of advantage to some players, however the game is very good so I don’t think much about this.

The rules are a 40 pages monstrosity, but I enjoyed reading them :D. I must be a masochist. The game is not really that hard but it does have a lot of rules so the game is not for the casual gamer. 

The gameplay is great, you have a lot to consider and the decisions always are hard, you can only play 4 cards per year so you always feel the tension in your decisions. The game has a lot of alliances, betrayals and “take that” moments. :devil: It has resource management, control area and lots and lots of battles. 

The battle system is very well done, it eliminated dice and added the fate card deck. I´m not sure if I would have preferred dice but I still like it a lot. 

I feel the heroes don’t play a critical role in the game, in my second play we played with the exploration tiles variant which I feel it is a must, otherwise the heroes feel very plain and boring to me and detract from the fun. I will delve further in this point in later plays.

Building the map felt a little like a chore, furthermore if you have to do it again after making some mistake.

In conclusion, Runewars is an epic fantasy game which I enjoy enormously, if you like that kind of thing give it a try.

Current Rating: 8.5
[hr]
2013-03-04

I’m not sure anymore that the Exploration tiles variant is the way to go. It adds a lot of uncontrollable randomness. Also, I’m downgrading its rating so my feelings are in concordance with the BGG rating system. Still feel this is a very good game, just not one I want to play all the time. 

Current Rating: 7.5

Games with lots of downtime have been (slowly) purged from my game collection. I also don’t enjoy “take that” games anymore so my desire to play Runewars is low.
 
Runewars is no longer among my favorite games of 2010.

RATING: 10
1st impression, but this is the best game I've played in a while. It's an "epic" game like Twilight Imperium, but I am much more enthusiastic about Runewars for the following reasons:

1) it appears to play much quicker than TI 
2) it seems like it will play very well with two players.
3) it appears to promote conflict. 
4) it has "role selection" of sorts, but I can select whatever role (action) I wish.  I'm not limited by the roles (actions) that other people select.  I tend not to like games where I can't choose some action just because someone else did.

I've played a few more times and my rating of the game goes up to a 10.  I think some people get a poor impression of the game because they try to buld a large empire instead of trying to achieve the victory conditions.  The game rewards agressiveness.  With the standard rules, the game is over relatively quickly.  You have to keep going after the runes whenever possbile.  I lost my last game by one turn because I hesitated instead of attacking.

RATING: 6.9
Another RTS computer game boardgame:  you collect resources and build units and fight.  Pretty simple, but Runewars adds the heroes dimension, in the quest for a better endgame condition than dominance.  It mostly works, but the end can be abrupt.  I like it.  The action cards and the dials are the innovation here - both a bit gimmicky.  This is not a really long game - should take around 3 hours.  Battle resolution is also gimmicky and I am less convinced with this bit but its a noble attempt at a thematically evocative combat system which can be resolved quickly.  The main problem with the heroes is that they add downtime.  Tiles are very pretty but a bit 'busy' in the artwork - its a bit overwhelming and can make it hard to pick the units out from the board - a lighter colour might have worked better.  I think Mare Nostrum is still the leader in the 'RTS' boardgame but this is a solid entry too.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
For multiplayer conflict games Runewars is as good as they get and I wouldn't mind joining in again. It has enough well-combined elements such as the order mechanism and the tactical cards to allow a player clever moves to sneak in the win. It's not too much free-for-all leaderbashing and the hero system introduces a nice counterbalance to simple military might. I'm just not crazy for this type of game. After one game I rate it a bit below my two benchmarks for this genre, Civilization: The Boardgame and A Game of Thrones. This is due to the Runewars fantasy setting that's not up to my taste and the ridiculous coffin-sized box and toylike components. In terms of gameplay, I wouldn't prefer any of them to each other without more experience.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Playing the revised rules with the original edition; haven't really done much with the expansion, which is partially obsolesced by the revision. My initial impression was that the game was a pretty good 4x game with perhaps a bit too much rules overhead. Now having played it six times, including twice recently and once with the "best" four, I largely stand by my description. The resource-gathering mechanism (via resource dials) greatly streamlines what in most games is a length, repetitive round-by-round resource count. The orders system worked great in A Game of Thrones to reduce decision overhead, and it works pretty much equally well here--constraining without impairing. The combat system arose during a period when FFG knew that resolution could effectively be done with cards *or* dice but not, in my opinion, cards *and* dice, and while some would presumably prefer a dice-based mechanic, the range and control of options, and swings of randomness mitigated, by the use of the small combat deck. So why not a higher rating?

While the game isn't heavy or overly long for what it is, I find the effort in prepping, retaining, and teaching a bit much (and there's usually a lot of that because we go for a while between plays). The Terrinoth theme is fine, I guess, and its genericness not really an impediment in a game so focused on broad fantasy tropes, but the factions are, I find, really dull, particularly the humans', and the "evil" factions names and unit types are painfully adolescent in their ersatz Tolkeinism. I'm not sure whether the red faction is entirely balanced with the others in terms of units; maybe the expansion addresses this in its unit additions.

On the revision: I think I might prefer the revised edition terrain (sans the cheapy vacuform hills that don't want to stay in place and weren't used in the expansion anyway), with its smaller, more manageable/storable pieces, and I would, of course, prefer the very minutely updated/errata'd content, but I have to give credit to FFG for tweaking without requiring an update kit (or a revised-edition purchase) for first-edition owners. I think the revisions are almost unequivocally improvements to what was already a solid game (and was far from broken). Most adroitly addressed--something borrowed from the expansion--was the victory condition; in revised, players must hold the requisite number of runes for a year to claim an early victory (and the runes are moved out of home realms, pushing players into greater conflict). I'm honestly indifferent on the role of heroes in battle (haven't yet tried the Commanders of the Battlefield variant from the expansion). It's mostly harmless, and it's kind of thematic in a Conan-y sort of way, but it addresses a problem that wasn't really there to begin with, in my opinion, but was perceived so by some players--namely, that there wasn't enough for heroes to do. I kind of like the separateness of the two "games" going on--armies and heroes--but, again, it's not a dealbreaker either way for me.

RATING: 8
To me, this falls in the same category as Twilight Imperium.  It's a bit shorter, although still very long (I'd say 1h + 1h / Player) and long to setup too.  But plays is a bit lighter/friendlier then TI3.  

The length is capped, there is good theme and naration (through events, quests, heroes, etc.) and solid resource management / euro style action selection while clearly remaining a wargame.  Combat is nice, descriptive and satisfying.  Everything is streamlined without loosing depth.  Of course, Fantasy Flight managed to "summarize" 15 pages worth of rules in 40 pages of text, but hey, at least it's clear and it's sound.  Oh, and lets not forget the bits & pieces: this game is simply gorgeous.  I mean, mountains!

I have a hard time giving super high ratings to long (4h+) games and also to wargames in general.  This being said, both the long game and wargame categories, this certainly gets my vote.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This review is based on reading the rules and closely watching the first half of a game in progress. I also own the original Battlemist game, so I will sometimes make comparison to it.

If you like other heavy and brain burning FFG games like TI3 and Starcraft, you might actually like this game.  If not beware.

Personally, I think the game progress way too slow. There are to much stuff to do and think about to do something. Which in the ends makes a game that there is a lot of work for little emotional reward to the player.

All these stuff that must be done consist is selecting action, making bids and playing cards to eventually get the opportunity to move somewhere, attack somebody or build something. I personally prefer game where your units have much more freedom on their movement.

Another weird thing is that they replaced combat dices with a deck of random cards. I know that there is various possible results (damage, rout, special ability), but the original battlemist game supported the idea of routing. Is it too complicated to implement it with dices. The system works, but for me rolling dices seems more interesting.

Compared to battlemist, there has been a few improvements. For example, the resources dial makes resource management much easier. Having a map with groups of hexes also makes it easier to build the map at the beginning of the game.

Still, they have removed some feature from the original battlemist and it's expansion. For example, you cannot travel by ship. I am sure it would have added a good dimension to the game and prevent players to get locked in their corners. It is not possible to use heroes in battle, that's another thing that could have been interesting. There is only 4 players compared to the original game that had 6. Maybe they will add players in an expansion.

There does not seems to be various path to victory conditions. What was interesting in the original battlemist game is that, where was multiple ways to get stars of timmoran and in the expansion, you had a completely new victory path. In rune wars, there seems to be only 1 way to win, control rune stones.

So owning battlemist and being a fan of the "master of magic" video game, I was wishing that I could get a good game that I would like. Unfortunately it does not match my taste. I am not surprised that FFG have made a heavy game like that since that is what they do with most of their games these days. It seems that all games designed by Corey Konieczka have that curse.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
An epic, heavy and complex fantasy area-control war game.

Gameplay:
+ complex, yet intuitive and interesting mechanics
+ unique races, with unique unit abilities
+ tactical and competitive
+ various ways to win the game (not only through combat)
+ well balanced in my experience
+ randomized map-set-up, quest cards, events, etc.

- the Heroes and everything concerning them seems to have no real value to the game. they don't contribute much to battles and outside of battles they basically travel around completely independent from the rest of the game
- the fate cards should have been replaced with custom dice for convenience

Components:
+ high quality components
+ A LOT of content in the box
+ great artwork
+ four unique races with separate miniatures

RATING: 9
A lot of people, including me, probably did not need to hear more than FFG Big Box and Battlemist reinterpretation to put this in a top spot on their to-buy list. Reading the rules beforehand only whetted my appetite, especially the seasonal approach to the game rounds and the short and sweet combat system.

Since Runewars was one of our most-played heavies last year, the automatic buy turned out to be a good decision. What I find most remarkable about the design is that it handles an epic strategic scope within a playing time that is still compatible with leading a life in the real world. Full four-player games play out within four hours, and as we’ve recently discovered, a one-on-one match can be done within 90 minutes, setup included.

The strongest point of the game might be this: even if you get horribly mangled, you will almost always have a "Yes!" moment during the proceedings. Whether its a battle won against overwhelming odds, a dragon that, perceiving your innate superiority, decides to join your cause or a particularly clever strategic ploy that works out as planned, something good will happen to you. Probably a lot of bad stuff, too, because there will only be a single winner at the end, but still - something good will happen.

And the sheer variety of stuff that happens, with all the seasonal events and tactic cards and unexpected turns of the tide and whatnot, ensures that no two games have played out the same so far. In fact, we’ve not yet felt the urge to try either the longer game variant nor the optional adventuring places for the heroes. In time, we’ll introduce these and possibly will also have a look at the inevitable expansions, but be assured that this is a very complete and rich game in its original form.

(-) way to less cards (i.d. quests, events, secret objectives)

RATING: 7
I hope it is out in time for me to put under the X-mas tree for myself! 

Wow.... I will wait to play it before I say it is a good game; but man it sure is pretty to look at. Corey K has had a good track record, I worry a bit that it will not be different enough from Ti3, but just a bit. 

1st play. Give this one my usual 7 after one play. Certainly lighter than TI3 and also a lot faster. I can see this being a 4 hr game even with the epic rules after we get the system down.

The fate deck could have been 2x as large. We were rolling through this thing a lot. 

I admit the complains are nit picking but they should also be easy to remedy and I expect to see them in the inevitable expansion.
