=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Loving the Terrinoth realm, Runewars has an easy path to my heart. But the game itself is really well put together. Playing through the seasons adds some variance, the simultaneous action card selection makes every turn interesting (not to mention managing your supremacy bonus), even the heroes plodding around the map to complete quests all come together to make a delectable package.

There is plenty of action, plenty of decisions, and plenty of bluffing to be had in this game. Probably among the best 2 hours of gaming you can do with a group.

Lets not forget about the different map tiles and random tile placement and the neutral monsters!

Fab game, one of my recent favorites.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.8
An excellent game, if a bit bogged down by having to look up rules and rule exceptions every 20 min (but then I've only played 8 times over a few months, so maybe after a dozen plays it'll all flow smoothly; I could aim that criticism at most FFG games I own, e.g., Fury of Dracula).  Still, if you're playing a 2p game that lasts for 3-4 hrs and you're still having fun, they've certainly done something right.

The heroes part of the game almost runs separately from the rest of it, so if you were teaching the game to new players (or wanted a faster version) you could probably just leave the heroes out entirely.

Great fun with 2 and 3!  [i]Played simulated 4p with two of us--Team Evil vs. Good Guys--a couple times, which was fun, but I still want a real 4 player game, and more 3 player games![/i]

Variants we use:
 - [b]Original win condition[/b] of 6 dragon runes, using false runes
[b] - Free Cities, which makes cities powerful and ups win condition to 7 runes
 - Expansion units[/b] for each faction
[b] - Expansion heroes[/b] (which integrate seamlessly)
 [b]- New terrain
 - Combat: Hexagon units[/b] count as standing even if routed during "Tally Strength" step
[b] - Combat: Hero Support[/b] allows extra fate card draw during battle
 - Most expansion tactics cards, quests, season cards (2 of 3 for each season), and reward cards.[/b]  We don't use anything with desolation or cryomancy tokens, so I've taken out the corresponding irrelevant cards.

Banners of War is a good expansion, but not necessary unless you play quite a bit.  However, it is nice to have 20 heroes instead of 12.

Download the Universal Head rules summary if you haven't--it's in the files section.  He does great work.

[I]*Own Banners of War expansion.[/i]

RATING: 7
After 5 games (3 4P and 2 3P) I favor the 4P significantly.  I am still pondering my rating since it might vary based on how many are playing.  Will try 2P and then use the epic variant also.

Learning game was long and somewhat painful.  All games since have been excellent and the time to play has decreased.

This game incorporates stuff from: Warrior Knights, Puerto Rico, MtG, and many more  (the good parts in my view).

Overall this game is a 9 or 10 for me.  I am hesitant on the 10 since the results after 11 games are a little more 'random' (hate saying that) than a game like Starcraft.  The exploration tokens didn't change that - they might have heightened it.  We will try the epic version for a while to see if that changes anything.

Now, after 20+ games the rating is still a 9.  This game is a blast to play (about 10 3p and 10 4p).  The tempo is excellent.  But, like WotR some little things keep this off the 10 rating.  The fate deck being tiny is very, very annoying - a large deck was warranted.  When shuffling a lot I prefer larger vs. smaller due to my physical issues.

Highly recommended for people who don't mind a little chaos and enjoy a fast-paced epic-scaled game.  Easily met my high expectations for it - another great game by the FFG folks.

Early 2014 - sadly downgraded due to the replayability issue.  This game needs at least 2 more factions to allow a much larger variety of options.  Sadly, it appears FFG doesn't think this needs anything new.

RATING: 8
I like this game a lot, but after four plays it is safe to say that I suck at it. I tend to play a conservative, military-oriented strategy, which produces strong defensive positions and impressive armies but doesn't get you many runes. I have so far undervalued the hero/questing portions of the game, much to the delight of my opponents.

I'm not sure whether this means that I like the military aspect of the game more or merely underestimated the importance of the hero/questing subgame. I think it's a little of both.

I have some minor criticisms of the game, the most significant of which is that some of the ways of acquiring a rune seem a bit squirrely. Most of these criticisms, however, are so minor as to be essentially irrelevant. In other words, there's nothing "wrong" about them, they're just either not what I expected or not what I preferred. They still work quite well, though.

It's a beautiful game to behold, that's for sure. Mmmmm, lovely, lovely, lovely. I finally couldn't help myself and bought a copy, even though I doubt it'll get played at home. I can just pull it out and stare longingly at the bits.

RATING: 8
Pretty entertaining 4 player game that really does not overstay.  Lots going on on the table and behind the scenes. interesting use of 'no dice' in a war/political game with the fate deck resolving everything.  

Can feel a bit gamey when players are attempting to stealthily acquire rune stones to win at the end of the game, but not too problematic.  It does open up the possibility for come from behind wins.

I really like how everything just fits.  I think there might should be another title in the game, but the game is clearly ripe for a stellar FFG expansion (but PLEASE make a Middle-Earth expansion first).

Does feel like there might should be a bit more for heroes to do, but they can clearly be used to enhance your position and can be used to grab rewards from other heroes via duels to cash in for runestones and/or do some runestone recon/etc.

RATING: 8.9
Birthday gift 2010 from the wife.

Brilliant design that seems to take the best bits from Warrior Knights, Twilight Imperium, even Chaos In the Old World (at least the variable player power bit) and boil them down into a refined core.

At first it felt like the Hero mechanic was a bolted-on and unnecessary complication, but in our first game it was the difference between first and second place - the winner being the player who exploited the Rewards to generate not one, but TWO Runes and push himself into the six realm.

I am very much looking forward to playing it again, and would even be interested in trying it with two.

--------------------------------------------------

Second play, three player game - completely tense and tight, and featuring a come from behind victory by the winner, where he swang from having three Runes, to six in one epic turn due to careful play of his quest rewards. I am starting to think duels are WAY more important that I originally thought, since the game is almost made in some instances based on cashing in quest rewards for extra rune tokens. Had he NOT done that, I would have take the victory right then and there by achieving my objective.

+.5 ratings bump. Next to try: two players.

-----------------------------------------------------

Ok A two player game where we punched each other in the face by turn two, and by turn four had torn each other to pieces. I eked out a victory by managing to gain a final Rune in a last ditch push into enemy territory, and it felt truly epic. I think if the starting player placement is right, the Undead are my favorite race to play right now, and the two player game is just about everything I enjoy about the game itself, condensed down into a knife-fight in a narrow alleyway. Another +.5 ratings bump. I cannot wait to play this one again.

--------------------------------------

This is getting constant play, and each experience is not only satisfying, new strategic depths are being revealed about the game - I have almost perfected playing the undead, and I have watched the clever ways that the elves turn their weaknesses (low starting food) into strengths. We are all now at a point where we can see the hero phase for what it is--the lynchpin of the game, and we now see three or four duels per game as heroes compete to steal each other's treasures. This game deserves another .5 ratings bump.

____________________________________________________

Continues to reveal depths in repeated plays. Fantastically intense with a group of four who know the game inside and out. Feels like it synthesizes the best of the current FFG catalog and becomes something greater than the sum of its parts. Getting nonstop play.

--------------------------

Ratings downgrade. Hits the table less and less, which is disappointing. I love it, it will never leave my collection, but I think my tastes are evolving beyond what it offers.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
In this epic big box game, players are attempting to earn 6 dragon rune tokens. The game lasts a maximum of 6 years. Each year, in each of the four seasons, players must decide which card from their 8 card hand to play. Cards allow you to move, recruit and harvest. If you play a higher numbered card than the previous season, you are allowed a bonus. You also control heroes that move around the board to complete quests. There are neutral creatures seeded on the board, and you can attack them or use diplomacy to get them on your side. Attacks, dueling heroes and resolving diplomacy is all done from a draw of cards. 

There are tons of cards and modifiiers, but they all seem to fit together reasonably well into this 4-5 hour game. It is a wargame, and you do have to target your opponents. If you enjoy the fantasy element, have 5 hours to spend, buckle up and enjoy the ride. 

It is a wargame, so you have to be in the mood for attacking, being attacked (sometimes unfairly) and using tactics. It is also a long game, though you are engaged the whole way.

**House Rules:**

No Generals. Instead the general trait cards are additional rewards for successful quests, combined with the new hero rules in the second edition.

Every second successful quest gives the character a leadership card they can use in battles in addition to the normal hero effect for heros when fighting from the second edition.

RATING: 8.8
And I was just thinking to myself, I should get Runebound.  It's a fantasy exploration game of that old Talisman style, the kind I miss from my childhood.  Man, am I glad I waited a week.  This game obliterates it.

Runewars takes the best parts of a LOT of Fantasy Flight Games -- and a LOT of other games as well -- and jams them together into an interesting area-control wargame with a ton of strategy, a little bit of luck, and no dice.  It's easy to lose focus on the winning conditions, but I saw three hours blow right past me and I was completely engaged in 99.44% of the game.  You're even engaged in the downtime as you watch your opponents battle, trying to strategize where to head next after this battle.

Some of the cards are lopsided.  Some of the extras didn't need to get added.  And it's WAY too expensive.  But all in all, this is a hell of a game here.  Forget Starcraft the Board Game... this is Heroes of Might and Magic right here, or Warlords II.  And it's fantastic.

RATING: 10
The aspect on Runewars I enjoy the most is its progressive pacing that seems to occur as the seasons pass. Initially each faction is essentially scrambling for a foothold in relative safety, but by years 2 or 3 it quickly becomes apparent this town isn't big enough for everyone. The tension as you push further out of your comfort zone trying to snag just one more rune becomes more and more frantic and by the last year turns usually dissolve into a massive explosion of chaos as everyone throws the kitchen sink into the mix trying to make that final push home to win. A consistently exciting and enjoyable game in my group.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.6
Love the heck out of this game. Hard to sell my wargaming buddies on it though, since it's not a wargame yet looks and acts like one and has the word "war" in the title.

I think this is an easy game to misunderstand, and I blame FFG entirely for this. They have a singular talent for setting the wrong expectations on good games (I'm frowning at you, Android), thereby making good games work harder than they should for fans.

Do heroes "matter"? Yes of course, they're one of the three primary ways you get runes, but they're not military units and don't really interact with the game at that level. So that's weird ... but only if you came to the game expecting that your hero bits "should" be military units.

Does conquest "matter"? Yes of course, but only if the other guy needs to harvest and/or you're taking his runes away from him. But conquest itself is not a path to victory.

I dunno...I think the wheels-within-wheels of this game are pretty great. I like the mass combat system, and sort of wish Twilight Imperium would consider a similar system. I like how Influence works. I like how Heroes work! It all comes together in this interesting pastiche of bits I've seen throughout FFG's catalog. It's like a mix tape of my favorite mechanics that stands on its own, like a good mix tape should. (Anyone younger than 35 will have no idea what I'm on about.)
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
BANN.
Expansions: 
1. Banners of War
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
A big sprawling territorial combat game, but with a twist. There's two games going at once. The heroes wander about fulfilling quests and trying to get more VP tokens. Whilst your armies expand your territory out so you have enough space to hold and protect the VP tokens gained. And/or go conquer someone else's VPs if you can. Tricky though because each unit can only commit to one combat each year, and there's only 6 years - they're armies, not marauders. The problem is that these armies and their combats don't achieve a lot by themselves as a result, just gradual expansion, but the time taken to work through the big battles feels too long for their contribution to the game to warrant. As well as introducing downtime to non-combatants. Although the initiative / card based system is clean, it would have been nicer to resolve these more quickly and get on the with the game. I guess it might result in a loss of epic-ness somehow though. Each to their own. The game is a big effort to learn because there's lots of moving parts (although most are intuitive). I'm just not sure that there's enough immersion and enough interesting decisions to warrant that effort and the time required to play.

RATING: 8
I must say, that I was quite surprised by this game. I tend to be more of a Euro player, but I found that the mechanics in this game lead to a smooth and fairly strategic game. There are no dice, instead an event deck that determines quests, skill checks and battles. In addition, you only have eight possible actions each turn, so there is less downtime then on many games of this type. The battles are fast and generally won by the larger army, yet the deck does provide some luck/surprise.

RATING: 7
I'm usually not a huge fan of multiplayer wargames--especially ones that take forever--but the ease of play of this one drew me in.  In this game you're trying to build up rune tokens instead of directly eliminating opponents, but since these tokens are tied to terrain, conflict will ensue.  Players have a hand of 8 action cards of which they will play 4 every "year", with bonuses given when the cards are played in a particular order.  Each action in itself is pretty simple, so the game doesn't get bogged down in administration.  Combat is card-based, which can get a little clunky.  Overall, probably one of the better epic multiplayer wargames I've played, though not something I'd get to the table very often.

I think I really wanted this to feel like Shogun with a fantasy theme.

RATING: 8.5
The first thing you'll notice is how amazing everything looks. The components are durable and beautifully decorated.  The small cards are actually flexible, making them easy to shuffle (unlike Arkham horror's cards).  The units have great sculpts.  I love the resource dials.  Everything has the 'weave' design.  The game has excellent play value, because there is so much going on: you have resource management, empire building, hero questing, neutral unit negotiations, and other things like dealing with the wizard's council with influence.  The order cards are sooo fun to pick out, planning your year's moves in advance and having to sometimes change your plan based on how the seasons of the year pan out.  The amount of strategy involved is amazing.  Well worth getting.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5
Light-ish Fantasy war game with (thankfully) not many dry, overwrought Euro mechanics that plague many of Fantasy Flight's games.

The Euro mechanic is in the order cards: On your turn, you pick from one of eight order cards that you cannot use again for the current year (there are four turns per year). Depending on the order in which you play your order cards, you may receive some bonus.

I say light-ish since strategically -- it takes maximum 2h to finish a game -- the game is straightforward but there are a lot of fiddly moving parts in the form of special action cards of a multitude of types that are acquired in a multitude of ways. It's unfortunate that a game this light has a relatively steep learning curve. The poorly written and organized rules don't help either.

I also appreciated the easily resolved combat mechanism, meaning that there is no card-based combat like the new Dungeon Quest that simply wastes time. 


=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This is the only fantasy battle game you'll ever need.  The game is simply gorgeous to look at and the components are top notch (I was surprised at the small size of the figures, but they are still very well sculpted). I like that there are multiple paths to victory and that it isn't all fighting.  I think the heroes are very well implemented.  They can definitely contribute to victory, but are not the focus of the game.  The game is not overly complex rules-wise, but has enough depth that it will take a few plays to explore the different strategies available.  If you're expecting a fight to the finish you may be disappointed.  You must keep an eye on who is getting runes and how, as the winner is not necessarily the one with the most military might.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
One of my favorite games, though I do recognize it's not the greatest game by any means.

I really like the dynamic in this game which I have never seen done before.  You're given hero(es) and armies.  Your hero is tasked with questing, which will most likely result in finding dragon runes.  Your army is tasked with both protecting the dragon runes you have found and possibly trying to claim your opponent's dragon runes.  Gaining a certain amount of dragon runes will win you the game.

The good:
The game has a very original dynamic and feel.
The game is nail-biting thematic, especially for hero fights.
A lot of the mechanics in this game aren't really seen anywhere else in the thematic board gaming world.

The bad:
Extremely long game, especially for how random it can be.
Very random (though there is randomness mitigation).
Lots of down time when playing with 3, and especially with 4.
Game end can come very suddenly (when playing without the new rule modifications).  For a really lengthy game, for it to just end very unexpectedly, can be a real deal breaker for some people.

Overall, I recommend this game if you like lengthy thematic games.  Good luck trying to find another person willing to play.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[b]RO[/b]: [i]12/16/2016[/i]

Runewars, Runewars... *clucks tongue* I loved you when I first purchased you from the now defunct Boards & Bits several years back. In your enormous coffin box, since replaced by a more reasonable sized container in the new edition. Your vast, empty confines with your plastic mountains and tiny army miniatures. Your seasonal events and awkward hero mechanics. The glazed look on the eyes of everyone I've explained this game to, your relatively small number of plays despite the intimidating shadow you cast over my game room with your enormity.

Runewars, you're not a bad game, you're actually kind of interesting. But grand fantasy military engagements are not what you're made of. Perhaps a few, pivotal and decisive engagements before a winner becomes apparent. I doubt you'll see much more play, moldering on your place on top of my shelves (because you are far too large to go [i]in[/i] the shelves), and I'll never trade you (because the postage would bankrupt us). You stand as a warning about impulse buys, a cautionary tale and a remnant of the early golden age of hobby gaming where giant boxes filled with plastic were still part and parcel of the hobby.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
A very good game. Compared to my other point of reference (Starcraft) for big FFG games with many plastic pieces, it is much more accessible because you don't need to plan out a path of development (which requires knowledge of how the different Starcraft technologies interact). Instead, you are responding to the cards you receive (Quest, Objective, Season and Tactical Cards), and thus it is a much more tactical game than Starcraft (which is more strategic). The influence auctions and heroes add another layer of competition which means the game is not all about conquest. The fact that you need to accommodate the runes, however, in a conquered territory means that there is a quite subtle interaction between the different elements of questing (heroes), conquest (armies) and politics (influence actions). The seasonal events are an excellent method of propelling the action, meaning you can't just sit back and turtle, and add a great deal of variety. The orders system packs much of the foreplanning of Starcraft into a much swifter and easier to teach mechanism that creates a lot of tension.

My only complaints are that I would have preferred having a whole order card devoted to moving heroes rather than just have them all move in summer; this way you would really have a clearer choice between a military and questing route to victory. The ending can also be somewhat anticlimatic, but that's mainly if the players aren't keeping track of who's been picking up the runes. Those who dislike chance disrupting their best-laid plans should also avoid, but personally I find this highly thematic and plausible.

Sold on e-bay.

RATING: 4.5
I was very disappointed with this game. This game has loads of potential, unfortunately for me it falls flat in many areas. Pretty much every critical review on here seems to hit the nail on the head for me as to what I don't like about this game. This game is poorly refined and has many awkward rules and game mechanics that don't seem to have a point or suit the theme.

The whole goal of the game is to obtain Dragon Runes. The runes ultimately serve as a mechanic to create conflict between player armies. I found it disappointing that there is no game play benefit for possessing these runes. The concept of Dragon Runes doesn't add much substance to the game, their implementation feels generic and tacked on and doesn't add anything to the theme. 

There are many aspects to this game that seem to be rather abstract and lacking polish. Combat is one-dimensional, each unit figure receives only one opportunity to attack during a combat round. This limits the amount of punishment and badassery any one unit can dish out; which I find to be rather anticlimactic. So the killer dragon you recruit has a huge chance of never even dealing damage in combat (and that's if you manage to get it to the front lines). The one round combats wouldn't be so bad if a battle would last more than one season. It would add to the epic theme of the game if a battle had the potential to last through the spring to fall or even longer. It could give you the opportunity to send reinforcements to the front lines. So many tactical options could become available if the game devs would have tweaked the combat system just a little.

Ultimately, aside from the many convoluted rules, underdeveloped themes, and weak combat mechanics, it is a pretty and fun game. To me many aspects of this game just fall flat and leave me wanting more.

RATING: 8
My favorite epic game. Even in the 'epic' variant (I don't play the standard game which is too short) plays under 5-6 hours, which is the most I'm prepared to spend on a single game. A lot is going on in this game, and while in Warrior Knights all that chrome detracted from the war on the map, here the balance between all the mechanics is just right for me. Not very much a thinking & diplomacy game like Game of Thrones is, it strides into adventure game territory where you play as much for living the story as for winning.

My review: http://boardgamegeek.com/thread/719526/insight-into-runewars-an-epic-adventure-strategy-g

RATING: 7
I found one 4 player, 5 hour play of this (epic rules, expansion included) extremely enjoyable, although I have a few reservations about replayability. Only fully realized the importance of questing towards the end of the game (a common mistake I understand); the randomness here, that one player could feasibly get 3 runes through the course of the game (or more?) whilst the others keep drawing nothing but duds has the potential of making a sideshow of everything else. If a player doesn't need to attack to get Runes then they could probably turtle with great effect. Nonetheless, it was great fun, I pretty much liked how all the various mechanisms played - simultaneous action selection, the dilemma of choosing action order, card combat, and so on. As is often the case with FFG, the mechanisms were all half familiar from other games - elements here of Game of Thrones, the first Warcraft boardgame, Runebound-in-miniature, Starcraft TBG simplified: For me, this is a great mix. I am very likely to swallow my reservations and spring for the reprint when it comes out, even if it only ends up seeing play once every year or so.

RATING: 6
A more convoluted version of [i][url=http://www.juegosdemesacr.com/cms/index.php?option=com_content&task=view&id=329&Itemid=1]Warcraft: The Boardgame[/url][/i], which I prefer, with some elements added from Fantasy Flight Games' prior games, like [i][url=http://www.juegosdemesacr.com/cms/index.php?option=com_content&task=view&id=143&Itemid=1]Warrior Knights[/url][/i] and [i][url=http://www.juegosdemesacr.com/cms/index.php?option=com_content&task=view&id=347&Itemid=1]A Game of Thrones[/url][/i].  Another example of overproduction not sustained in the depth or even in the needs of the game mechanics, but to justify and oversized price.  Even so, an OK game.  By the way, it looks like somebody told the FFG's staff that combat resolutions using dice mechanics aren't cool, even when they really are.  It is easier to plan with the cards and the results are somehow fairer, but the twist of the possibilities using dice are simply incomparable.

I think I'd enjoy the game more if it had no blind bidding, face up runes, more than one rune to a space, and a simplified Conquest of Nerath dice type battle mechanic.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
A bloated monster of a game. It lacks the one brilliant idea in Corey's other works like Battlestar Galactica (skill checks) and Middle-Earth Quest (life/damage/rest pools), but is remarkable for how it glues the kitchen sink together. The rules are extremely simple, basically resolve a random event for everybody, then everybody play an action simultaneously, but layered complexity makes decisions tough.

Nearly every area-influence, conquest game you can think of is here in some form. Exploration from Nexus Ops, blitzing from Axis & Allies, building and upkeep in Shogun, plus the threat of getting hit before you can exploit. And of course, A Game of Thrones appears to be the mechanical inspiration.

Still, a little too bloated, with all kinds of tiny cards lying around the table, some confusing rules exceptions, and weird dynamics (especially with a bad map) where the game can sometimes end before the battles get going. Battles can be weird -- losing a battle is harsh, but the game gives the advantage to the attacker, creating a lot of weird I-have-to-attack-lest-he-attacks-me dilemmas, which take getting used to. Not a fan of the Fate Deck, since it's very fiddly to pass it around a big table, and is a lot of work just to simulate fancy die rolls.

Two separate mini-games, with the army battles in one arena and heroes/adventure in the other, with limited, card-driven ways to interact between them. The heroes/adventure element is not fully fleshed out (move here to complete quest, boom, rinse, repeat), and can feel like a burden on the game sometimes when you have to spend 15 minutes every game year on the Quest Phase. Though it does provide a nice respite. Mixed feelings about it, but definitely would have preferred a way for heroes to lead in battle.

Lots of tiny annoyances, but you cannot beat the sheer epic scale of pushing dudes around and colliding in big battles. The highs are awesome and allow me to overlook the lows.

[hr]
A nice set of simple positive changes with the Revised Edition. Heroes are more mobile, making their abilities better and questing easier and more important. It, combined with heroes' leadership in battle, provides a simple way to increase interaction between heroes and armies, far better than the contrived Commanders module in the expansion. Road to Victory was sorely necessary. Defaulting everything to Epic Variant now really increases game length, but I do like that rune tokens start outside, encouraging early aggro and making the Commander of the Warriors Guild title better early-game.

Still prefer the expansion (and playing base again made me re-appreciate it), but I no longer mind just playing base. Bump from 8 to 9.

Note to self: play to fewer dragon runes in future. 1ed was 6 years to gain 4 w/o Road to Victory, while Revised Ed changed it to 7 years to gain 6 + Road to Victory. Think can easily scale it down to 4 or 5. Encourages conflict too.

Base: 4+5
 + BoW: 4+5

g{[b]Favorite 4-player Long[/b]}g

RATING: 7
[2 plays] I liked this. It is nicely produced, has some nice mechanisms, and feels like a cross between Nexus Ops and Warrior Knights. I will definitely play it again. The complicated decision tree that develops with so many different card types in hand after a while will scare away casual gamers - or at least it should. Runewars seems like it will probably always absorb the entire evening. Our first game went 90+ minutes for the rules and set-up, and then finished in the third out of six years, after about 3.5 hours of play. I'm not convinced the game really needs the hero component, but I'm also not convinced it would be satisfyingly superior to Nexus Ops without it in terms of game length/enjoyment ratio.

Most recent play 4/19/13.

RATING: 10
A fun game, lots of interesting components and strategies.  The order choosing system is more interesting than I expected - choosing a higher order to sacrifice future supremacy bonuses is something you really have to consider.  The combat is an interesting combination of luck and risk management - and done in a way that really couldn't be fully captured by dice.

Definitely worthy of the ranks of Twilight Imperium 3 and others of the higher-end Fantasy Flight games.

The more I play this game, the more I want to play "just one more time, I promise". :)

RATING: 10
I have moved my initial ranking of this game up to a 10. The more I play this game, the more subtle strategies I discover. I enjoy the strategy behind it, the different ploys you can try to win the game, and the back stabbery that the nations must undertake to win. 

I thoroughly enjoy the combat mechanic of the game. The only downside in my opinion is the common complaint of the fate deck. It gets old and somehow. I can't help but think dice would have been a better choice. That observation notwithstanding, the way the fate deck works and has been implemented for multiple mechanics in the game is very clever.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
[2013-03-09]
Egy újabb fantasy nagyágyút sikerült kipróbálni a Descent, Mage Knight vonalból. Mindkettőre hasonlít sok téren, van egy kis Stracraft rész is benne, de annyira nem nyűgözött le. Nézzük a pozitívumokat. Nagyon jó az erőforrásmenedzsment rész a játékban. Három nyersanyag különböző mértékű birtoklása mind a játékban játszható 4 fajt különböző előnyökhöz juttatja. A négy játszható faj nagyon jól kiegyenlített, igaz nem sok lényeges különbség van köztük, de jól eltaláltak. Tetszett a harcrendszer is mind a kártyás megoldás, mind az egységek jól eltalált fajtája és azok száma. Alapvetően akciókártyákat játszunk ki, különböző értékekkel, amelyek közül a kisebb számú kerül először sorra. Ebben semmi újdonság nincs, sok játékban megjelenik. Érdekesség, hogy minden kártyának van másodlagos képessége, ami akkor kerül végrehajtásra, ha nincs nála nagyobb számú kártya kijátszva. Ez jó kis ötlet. A játékban két dolog nem tetszik. Az egyik a cél elérése. Hat sárkányrúna birtoklása a saját területünkön. Ez ha megvan be kell jelenteni és egy évig tartani. Egy év négy körből áll. Nagyon sokféleképpen lehet rúnákhoz jutni, de sajnos sok az olyan eset is, amikor vesztünk belőlük. A játék végén már sokszor egy-egy jó kártya dönt, hogy sikerül-e tartani vagy nem. És akkor itt jön a játék nálam legnagyobb hibája a szerencsefüggősége. Mindenhol megjelenik. A csatakártyáknál még csak-csak tolerálható, de sok helyen már zavaró. A hősök az egységektől teljesen függetlenül mozognak a terepen és könnyedén szerezhetnek szerencsével rúnákat, míg mások elhalnak egy-egy küldetés nehézsége miatt. Egysíkú a játékmenet, minél több egység gyártása, kulcsterületek bevédése és a végén bízni a szerencsében, hogy meg tudjuk tartani őket a rúnáink számára. Nem rossz játék a Runewars, de nekem a Mage Knight jobban bejött és a Descent is változatosabb.

My biggest complaint is not the gameplay but the price and the box. There is the world domination game, the runebound-ish hero game, and then the hard to define runestone game.  There seems to be little correlation from any of these to the other.  In a 3 hour game the Winner is the person who happens upon the most runestones or gets lucky with an event or vote.  I have soured on this considerably.

RATING: N/A
RuneWars è un gioco da tavolo epico di diplomazia, combattimento e cerche per 2- 4 giocatori. Progettato da Corey Konieczka, RuneWars mette a confronto i giocatori in una sfida strategico di battaglie e di controllo di spazi vitali, dove devono raccogliere le risorse, riunire le loro armate e assediare città molto fortificate. RuneWars include oltre duecento carte magnificamente illustrate e altrettanti segnalini, così come oltre duecento miniature di plastica finemente dettagliate, anche se il punto di forza del gioco rimangono le mappe esagonali componibili. Presentando elementi tridimensionali in rilievo, le mappe assicurano che nessuna partita sia uguale alle altre.

RuneWars è ambientato nello stesso universo fantasy del noto gioco da tavolo Runebound e di Descent: Viaggi nelle Tenebre, e molti eroi e mostri ben conosciuti dai fan dell’ambientazione fanno qui il loro ritorno.
Le guerre per le rune dei draghi sono cominciate e solo una fazione sarà vittoriosa!

Elenco elementi di gioco:
36 Unità Neutrali in plastica di colore beige
36 Unità di Umani in plastica di colore blu
36 Unità di Non Morti in plastica di colore viola
36 Unità di Uthuk in plastica di colore rosso
12 Eroi in plastica di colore grigio
5 Fogli in cartoncino fustellato
4 Schede Riassuntive
56 Carte di grandi dimensioni
168 Carte di piccole dimensioni
12 Perni in Plastica
10 Montagne 3D in plastica

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Fun game with a lot of theme and flavor. Unfortuantely, there are a few mechanisms which I find pull the game down in comparison to something like Twilight Imperium III.

The whole hero going around doing quests is neat, but really distant from everything else that is going on. They move separately, act separately, and are completely split from everything else. Just feels like it was tacked on to make it fit in the Runescape universe.

Also, the items that heroes get are all over the place. Some are completely useless, while others are potential runes (and thus victory points). If someone manages to get nothing but runes through items, while another person gets other things, it can really hurt the game.

Finally, the "Objectives" are grossly imbalanced. Taking 7 hexes outside of your home base takes easily the entire game, while getting 2 Giants to join your side, or getting a hex next to an enemy base is relatively easy in comparison.

If only the game was better balanced and gave you more avenues to win outside of getting lucky with items or objectives, I think the game would be a lot better.

Still, I thought it was fun, and I definitely want to play it again!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Epic and random fantasy conquest game. Players take the roll of one of four factions (Humans, Elves, Undead and Uthuk), all of which play slightly differently from one another, in an attempt to conquer regions containing dragon runes. As the game progresses and more regions are conquered by a faction, the stronger they become due to a greater number of resources. Each turn consists of an order selection, which allows a certain action to take place e.g. movement, engage in conflict, reinforce etc. There is also a nice feature whereby factions have heroes who operate independently of their armies and attempt to complete quests in return for rewards. Overall 'Runewars' is an impressive game with a lot of commendable features (I particularly like the combat system which involves drawing cards and referring to symbols based on the units involved for results) but I do feel that there is one level of randomness too many here and too often has "I've got this card which..." moments, resulting in the game feeling far too dependant on luck.

RATING: 8
The first time I played Runewars, it was at a friend's place, and I wasn't entirely sure that this was my type of game. It was long, seemed fiddly and complicated - one of those confrontational games where someone else who knows better would just pound your armies to the ground. War games just aren't my thing.

And then, after the game, I found that I actually rather enjoyed it! I read a little more about the design ideas behind Runewars; the designer had wanted to capture the sort-of Macro/Micro feel of a fantasy story. Like LotR where you have armies of elves, humans and orcs clashing with each other, while on a micro-level, individual hobbits and heroes try to complete their missions which could affect the whole thing.

I really like Runewars, and I will handily teach and play it anytime.

RATING: 9
I am not so sure on how to rate this yet. I probably experienced one of the worst swings of bad luck I ever had in a boardgame. And though this event (which actually was a chain of events) is very unlikely, I am not so happy with the fact that the game permits this. Admittedly. there was a risk, but it was a very low one. However the effects were desastrous. 

Example: Humans with one cavalry and a fortress in their realm (Me, as the Orc attacking with 7 units, 3 Berserkers, 2 flesh rippers, and 2 chaos demons), fresh deck of fate cards. My flesh rippers fail to kill him, so the cavalry gets to attack and draws - guess what - one of two of the "Route 2"- cards in the deck of 30. 
Ok, I kill his cavalry but have to retreat since my strength of 5 only equals his. 
All my retreated units are routed. But since I didn't capture the stronghold the human - who played recruit that round - was now able to place armies in the fortress which should have been mine) and on the next turn plays a Tactics-card with which he picks up a beforehand played Conquest- or Mobilize-order (he had played both beforehand) card, thus now being able to attack my routed army (which has retreated into a stronghold region of my own) Due to the fact that he was attacked by another player (in a region where he already had an activation marker) and retreated from that fight, he was able to play yet another powerful card with which to place routed armies back to normal. And since these armies were not in an activated region anymore they were also able to join the fight against me. So not only did I not have a massive army and two strongholds in that (those two) regions, but ended up with an almost completely wiped out army and no stronghold. 
I know this is all written very confusing. It's just to give you a clue on the chain of negative events that did spoil my first experience with this game a little. However I still want to try this game again because I am convinced, I actually will enjoy it, because in the end this game has everything I like.  

Rating will probably go up.

Upate #1 from 6.5 to 7.5 - keep it coming. It's growing on me. 

Third game in - I like it more and more: 8.0

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
This is an epic fantasy game mechanically similar to Twilight Imperium but better realized. Thanks to the theme, terrain tiles are more varied (deserts, forests, volcanoes etc), very beautiful and have interesting obstacles like rivers that freeze in winter and can be crossed. Map setup is vastly superior as it can produce realms of any shape and cleverly balances starting positions. The game flows much better than TI because of new resource and unit recruitment system, which eliminates a lot of upkeep, simultaneous selection of actions via cards (a la RftG) and reducing the amount of troop movement (you can activate at most 2 sectors per year, and game lasts only 6 years). Winning condition (obtaining dragon runes) also has more logic than arbitrary points in TI, and the need to place runes in your sectors creates wonderful tension because they require protection and it encourages more conflict, which is awesome in my book.

It still inherits some flaws like annoying and random tactic cards with effects ranging from useless to game breaking, or combat that plays itself. But I find it much more elegant and enjoyable overall, both thematically and mechanically, so I recommend trying it even if you didn't like TI3.

RATING: 8
Runewars is an epic board game of conquest, adventure, and fantasy empires for two to four players. Runewars pits players against each other in a strategic game of battles and area control, where they must gather resources, raise armies, and lay siege to heavily fortified cities.
Runewars takes place in the same popular fantasy universe as the board games Runebound and Descent Journeys in the Dark, and dozens of fan-favorite heroes and monsters play their part. The wars for the dragon runes are beginning, and only one faction will emerge victorious.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Context: I traditionally like my decisions in a game to a more controlled impact as the length of the game increases. Runewars, for my group, is a 2-4 hour experience (from set up to pack down) and I consider this to be a "long" game.

I like this game.

I find that it fulfills on being an area-control, economic, strategic, civilization game. It scratches those itches really nicely, while providing some excellent decisions through the use of the Order cards. 
I would recommend playing this game to see if it suits you, because (as I have found, at least) it stands relatively alone in this area of the market. For what it does, I see few peers.
That is not to say the game is perfect. I have quibbles with the combat resolution (drawing cards and checking symbols) and would prefer something more in the control of the player. But, this would have increased the time-length and shifted the focus of the game. 
In short, do not go into this game expecting the combat to be a satisfying experience, it is a perfunctory distraction from the real meat of the game: The area-controlling, civ-building, economic, strategic engine.

I love making the decisions and every season that passes has my full attention. The hero phase (within the Summer season) is like a game within a game that I delight in. It gives the game layers and levels, but the caveat is that it can become distracting toward the later stages. 

I can't vouch for the replay value of the base game. But I can wholly say that the Banners of War expansion more than fills this gap. Acquiring this expansion took this game from being a cool part of my collection to a valued member. I wouldn't think of bringing the game out without at least one module in it from the expansion. 

All round thumbs up, the table-talk this game creates is excellent. A lot of banter and threats as secret agendas are laid bare each season.

Things seem pretty balanced between each of the races, to my knowledge, but it is INCREDIBLY important that you do not take short-cuts when setting up the map. Help new players make informed decisions or, like in simpler games like Catan, games will be won in the pick of starting positions. You will get accustomed to this, though. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
After one play with three, this game left me a little bit disappointed. The main reason for this, is that victory seems to rely quite a bit on luck. Like in most FFG games, there is a ridiculous amount of draw piles (and card drawing) involved, and many of the cards (especially the season and tactics cards) have a lot of influence on your winning chances. With three players, the weakest army was also continuously battered by the other two, leaving the impression that the game probably plays best with four. And then there is the insane amount of rules (40 pages no less!).
Despite all of this, the game is atmospheric and fun to play. Oddly enough, taking turns feels like a streamlined process (quite similar to Warrior Knights in fact), which will undoubteldy ensure a much faster second play. Downtime is not too bad, as players choose most of their actions simultaneously. The game also plays remarkably quickly, taking (in general) maybe three to four hours to complete. Components are good, but the box is mainly empty!
UPDATE: After a game with four players, I am strengthened in my believe that this is the optimal number of players. The game was more fun the second time, but I still find the random factor (and especially the potency of the tactics cards) pretty big, making for big (unpredictable) shifts in balance every now and then.

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Пока что все впечатления будут предварительными ввиду малого количества партий. В целом же я пока весьма доволен игрой.

1. Изначально меня останавливала перед покупкой излишняя пестрость карты - я ожидал что будет сложновато находить на ней ресурсы и вообще. В целом это оказалось справедливо, но не так страшно, как например в последней Цивилизации. (А уж оформление в целом так и совсем лучше. И даже бой несопоставим). Самый неудачный момент игры для меня - относительная сложность подсчета ресурсов, который время от времени предстоит выполнять. Также в недостатки запишу что на части карт всетаки нет картинок.

2. Если же сравнить Рунварс с декбилдинговой Рун Эйдж - которая мне понравилась всем, кроме декбилдинга - то Рунварс для меня выигрывает именно наличием игровой карты (вместо декбилдинга).

3. Итак, это конечно америтреш во всей красе, но как и в TI3, тут отмечается множество положительных вкраплений.

i). Во-первых игра идет на достижение целей (ТИ3), но тока более явно стремление отбирать нажитое у соседей. Отбирать не обязательно - есть выбор.

ii). Как и в ТИ3 - есть выбор ролей. Хотя пул ролей уже не общий для игроков, он ничуть не менее замысловатый. Совершенно другой короче, и тоже заковыристый. Завязан на сезоны кстати (смотреть далее)

iii). Очень много рандома, что тематично и просчитываемо. Например вы можете укрепиться в узком проходе между гор, а вражина используя спец-карту пройдет через горы в обход. Вероятность наличия такой карты можно оценивать.

iv). Сезоны. Не считая того, что это тематично и вписывается в механику (например зимой кормят армию и замерзают реки) - интересно работает связка с выбором ролей, а также каждый сезон приносит свое случайное событие (в сравнении с общим пулом в Игре Престолов).

v). Кстати о Престолах. Тут тоже есть титулы и голосования. Сбор "бочек" также навевает сравнения - только ресурсов уже 3 типа, а случайные события так и норовят все сделать еще хуже (например засуха может заставить немедленно проапдейтить "бочки", да еще с пенальти). 

vi). Герои и квесты. Умеренно хорошо. Не вызвало такого отторжения как скажем было в WoW Мир Приключений (с их "меч +1 если выпадет 6"). Скорее можно говорить о желании большего разнообразия, что возможно решается дополнением.

vii). Баталии. Я все же ожидал, что в них будет некий элемент Warrior Knights (или если хотите Покера), но все оказалось проще. Честно говоря не совсем понимаю почему - имхо с теми же картами сражения можно было сделать более впечатляющими (возможно этим займусь еще).

Итого. Жестко выбирая роли мы пытаемся находить или отбирать руны. Очень тематично, эпично, много случайных событий, конфликтно и напряженно. По времени выглядит приемлимо.

RATING: 7
Feels like a streamlined version of Twilight Imperium. The game could have been amazing, but there are some issues. First, I agree with the nay-sayers: There is a slight disconnect between the "main game" and the hero sub-game. Secondly, the game sometimes feels like it "hands" a player victory by allowing him/her to gain runes all over the place without the player even having a semi-coherent strategy. Still, gameplay is very smooth and combat fast. Looking forward to the expansion (and if the expansion does the same for Runewars as the TI3-expansion did for that game, Runewars might be amazing yet). 

Update: After a few more plays, I've increased the rating by 1. This game has grown on me. 

We usually play with an optional rule: Play lasts for six years, i.e. 24 seasons. When the game ends, count runes. A false rune is worth 1 VP, a real rune 2. The player with the most VP wins. The tie-breaker is the player with the most real runes (second tie-breaker: most influence).

Update: Rating down 1. No one I know has really fallen for Runewars. After I got Conquest of Nerath, I have been wanting to play that instead.

RATING: N/A
Runewars is an epic board game of conquest, adventure, and fantasy empires for two to four players. Runewars pits players against each other in a strategic game of battles and area control, where they must gather resources, raise armies, and lay siege to heavily fortified cities.
\13\\13\
Runewars takes place in the same popular fantasy universe as the board games Runebound and Descent Journeys in the Dark, and dozens of fan-favorite heroes and monsters play their part. The wars for the dragon runes are beginning, and only one faction will emerge victorious.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Intended to be a reboot of Battlemist, which was essentially a fantasy version of Twilight Imperium, this looked promising  to me. Twilight Imperium is amazing in scope, but the game hasn't aged all that well. It's too long, there's too much down time and all too often the game's action only involves two or three players when it is all said and done. If they could streamline this stuff, I was more than happy to bring this style game back to my tabletop.

Ironically, I think they took out too much. The card battle system is interesting, but it's also tedious. It also rewards card counting, which I understand is considered to be a feature by many, but I think it robs the battles of tension when the outcome is already known before the card is flipped.

Also, only including four races is laughable. There should have been at least eight in this box, to keep it interesting. Especially when you consider each race is somewhat pre-programmed via their unique unit selection to behave in a fixed way, when pursuing victory.

I'm also getting burnt out on Fantasy Flights obsession with printing tokens and cards with no words on them. Draw a card from the fate deck. Which one is that, none of them say FATE on them. Flip a token. It's a tiny picture of a desert, a swamp...or a plate of bacon. I really can't tell. I guess we'll have to look it up AGAIN. Seriously Fantasy Flight, enough with the token and crib sheet model and just print cards for everything already. Oh, and for the love of God, label them accordingly!

RATING: 8
I enjoy several aspects of this game. The battle system, although injected with a good deal of luck, is still fun and even 'workable', since you draw cards with symbols and hopefully get certain powerful symbols to damage the enemy. You can increase the odds of good draws in your favor by the amount of units you have to represent you in the battle.

Another thing I enjoy is the way this game makes confrontation eminent. Limited resources, which are needed for building strongholds and maintaining healthy troops, are found on the modular map tiles and give everyone a reason to conquer territory, along with the acquisition of cities and most importantly, dragon runes, which are what win you the game.

It is so much fun building troops and expanding terrain that I can sometimes forget that the real way to win this game is not how big or expansive your faction is, but simply how many dragon runes you have. 

The only 'drawback', is the length of play. I play only 2-player, and with full rules we average about 3 to 3 1/2 hours. But if I have that time to spend on this game, it is always a pleasure.

RATING: 6
Shorter and more fun than Warrior Knights.  Our game's highlight: Heroes dying randomly due to poor quest-related Fate card draws (landslides, murmuring fairies, dinner gone horribly wrong, etc.).

Combat is a let-down, though, in that there are no choices to make once the units are in place.  In Warrior Knights, at least you get to choose a smaller hand of cards from those that are drawn.

Of the recent Fantasy Flight games, Middle Earth Quest has the most satisfying card-based combat system - primarily because players have real decisions to make across multiple combat rounds.  Runewars battles, in contrast, are mindless one-round exchanges where the players feel like spectators.

RATING: 7
This is an odd one for me.  When I look at each individual element I really cant find things I like about the game. However when I add it all together I enjoy it and like playing it despite that.

In particular the combat seems more pointless than it should be. You can have a great army and win every battle and still finish in last place. The purpose of the combat it seems is to carve out enough space to store your victory points on which you can easily do with mediocre forces (the game ends before you can really reach your opponent and fight for his).  However to get the victory points is just some random quests -- making the game a light one despite the heavy systems for troop building and combat. 

Categorically its a strange game too (as are the others by this designer)  The mechanics are fun and novel but not thematic and dont work towards the theme in particular (which I can quite like in a game -- to have quirky fun mechanics that is) However past a certain minimal level of expertise the strategy is out of your control leaving you with a sit back and enjoy those quirky (but non-thematic) mechanics sort of ride.

RATING: 6
Massive box filled with Chinese air and a shitload of plastic.

System feels overburdened with waaay too many annoying decks of cards, and spinning plates. The rules governing the heroes are just awful and unthematic, and the most broken thing about the game is the negotiation aspect - which can lead to gargantuan swings in power.
